وامتناع كون المضافين كذلك بل أن يكون المعقول المحتاج إلى تعقل غيره لا يتقرر في الذهن ولا في الخارج إلا لأجل وجود ذلك الغير بإزائه . قوله : ( أي هو في حد نفسه إلخ بخلاف القسم الأول من المضاف المشهوري A note: His saying: 'The relative (al-mudaf) is that whose essence is intelligible in relation to another' is not intended to mean that conceiving the other is entailed [by it], for then the necessary concomitants (al-lawazim al-bayyina) would be such [i.e., they entail their subjects], so the essences with clear concomitants would enter into the definition of the relative. Rather, it is intended that from its reality it requires conceiving the other, so it cannot be conceived except by conceiving the other. That is, it is in itself such that its essence is not fully conceived except by conceiving something external to it. When that other is restricted to being a relation, the rest of the relations are excluded, and this statement includes both the real relative and the second type of the well-known relative. Complete. His saying: 'i.e., it is such that it entails etc.' So the text of the Matn is either based on deletion of the object of the relative, i.e., [it is] a necessary condition for the clear concomitants like the other in the necessity of their dependence [on the subject] due to the dependence of the subjects. His saying: 'From its reality [it requires] conceiving the other' contains a problem: if he means it is part of its reality, then the other is a part [such that] conceiving each of the two relatives depends on conceiving the other and its priority over it; if he means it arises from conceiving the reality of the other, then it is objected that the necessary concomitants of the essence are like that. Similarly in his saying 'it is not conceived except by conceiving' if the preposition 'bi' is taken as causal, priority is entailed; if taken as accompaniment, and the subjects [are considered] in relation to their clear concomitants, the expression is insufficient to express the intended meaning. The answer: The intended meaning [of 'bi'] is the completion of conceiving the other together with it because it is opposite to it, and the remaining [part of] his saying 'except by conceiving the other' is in the sense of 'with.' Its detail is in the Eastern Investigations (al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya) in agreement with al-Shifa: The meaning of 'the essence is predicated in relation to another' is that the essence requires in its conception the conception of something external. However, the subjects, when conceived, it is conceived with them that the essences of the subjects are not predicated in relation to the essences of the concomitants due to the necessity that the essence that is the subject or the necessary condition is independent in itself and prior in its essence to the concomitants, and it is impossible for the two relatives to be thus. Rather, the intelligible that needs the conception of another is not established in the mind nor in the external except due to the existence of that other opposite it. His saying: 'i.e., it is in itself etc.' Unlike the first type of the well-known relative, for it is not in itself like that, but rather by virtue of its accident. His saying: 'When that other is restricted etc.' And the author did not restrict it [in his definition] because his aim was to explain the meaning of being intelligible in relation to another. There follows: His saying: 'And these two are called well-known relative' in Sharh al-Maqasid: What occurred in al-Mawaqif that the subject alone is also called well-known relative is contrary to the well-known. Yes, it may be called mudaf in the sense that it is something that has idafa, in accordance with the linguistic convention. His saying: 'i.e., it is such that it entails etc.' The context requires that one say: the subjects with clear concomitants are like that. So the commentator pointed to the explanation mentioned by al-Abhari: that his word 'that' (dhalika) refers to the conception of the other, not to the composite. You may also interpret the author's expression as involving the deletion of the object of the relative, i.e., the things entailed by the concomitants. His saying: 'And when that other is restricted to being a relation' and the author did not restrict it here, relying on what preceded in the first observatory of this book.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage is a detailed analysis of the definition of a relative (al-mudaf). The commentator begins with a cautionary note: the well-known definition 'the relative is that whose essence is intelligible in relation to another' must not be misinterpreted as including necessary concomitants (al-lawazim al-bayyina), because those also entail conceiving something else (their subject), but the relative is different. The true meaning is that the relative's reality requires conceiving the other, such that its essence cannot be fully conceived without conceiving the other. When the other is restricted to being a relation (not any object), then the definition includes both the real relative and the second type of well-known relative (the composite). The commentator then examines the phrase 'from its reality it requires conceiving the other' and considers two possible interpretations: either the other is part of its reality, or it arises from conceiving the other. Both have problems: if part, it leads to circularity; if consequential, it collapses with necessary attributes. The solution is that the preposition 'bi' in 'except by conceiving' means accompaniment, not causation, and the relative's conception is completed by conceiving its opposite (muqabil). The commentator cites al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya to support this. He then distinguishes between the real relative (which is in itself dependent) and the first type of well-known relative (the subject alone, which is not dependent in itself but only by accident). Finally, he clarifies a point from Sharh al-Maqasid: calling the subject alone 'well-known relative' is not standard, but it can be called mudaf in a linguistic sense. The commentator also notes that the author did not restrict the definition to relation because his goal was to explain the meaning of 'intelligible in relation to another.'

Key Terms
المضاف
اللوازم البينة
تعقل الغير
المضاف الحقيقي
المضاف المشهوري
المقابلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Necessary concomitants are relatives because they also require conceiving another.
Correction: A necessary concomitant can be conceived without its subject (though it entails it), whereas a relative's essence cannot be conceived at all without its correlate.

Checkpoint
Why does the definition of relative (al-mudaf) exclude necessary concomitants?
A) Because they are not relations
B) Because they do not require conceiving another
C) Because they entail their subjects but the relative requires conceiving its correlate for its very essence
D) Because they are not intelligible

Key Takeaway
The relative (mudaf) is defined by its essence requiring conception of another, unlike necessary attributes which merely entail their subjects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a relationship you have that defines who you are (e.g., as a parent, child, spouse). According to this lesson, is that relationship part of your essence, or is it a necessary concomitant? How does the answer change the way you view yourself when that relationship is absent?
?
The passage warns against confusing a relative with a necessary concomitant. Are there aspects of your faith that you treat as 'necessary accompaniments' rather than essential parts of your identity? What would it mean for your connection with Allah to be truly essential to your conception of yourself, not just an attribute that follows?

Logic and Technical Terms

Two Properties of al-Mudaf: Parity and Reciprocity
States the two properties of al-mudaf: parity in existence and reciprocality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two essential properties of relational concepts in Islamic logic: parity in existence (takafu') and reciprocity (in'ikas), including how temporal relations fit within this framework.

Core Idea
Relative terms exist together or not at all, and each points back to its partner.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif explains the two fundamental properties of relational terms (al-mudaf). The first property is that whenever one relative exists, the other must also exist, and likewise for non-existence. This raises a puzzle about temporal relations like 'prior' and 'posterior'. The second property is reciprocity: each relative is defined by its relation to the other. Understanding these properties is key to analyzing relational concepts in logic and theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two properties
Read the passage to find the two explicit properties mentioned for al-mudaf. Notice that the first is about parity in existence and non-existence, and the second is about parity in relation (reciprocity).
The passage begins by stating that al-mudaf has two properties.
2
Understand the first property: parity
Focus on the definition of the first property: whenever one relative exists in mind or external, the other exists; and when one is absent, the other is absent. Consider why this is important for relational concepts.
The property applies to both real (haqiqi) and common (mashhuri) relatives.
3
Examine the objection about temporal priority
The passage raises an objection: how can temporal prior and posterior be relatives if they do not exist simultaneously? Read the answer carefully: they exist only in the mind as relational considerations, so parity holds in mental existence.
This is a common point of confusion that the author clarifies.
4
Grasp the second property: reciprocity
The second property is that each relative is predicated of the other inasmuch as it is relative to it. This means that to understand one relative, you must refer to the other.
Reciprocity ensures that the relation is mutual and necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الخامس - المقصد الثاني : خواص التكافؤ للمضاف.
أعني المركب ) وأما القسم الأول منه أعني المعروض وحده فليس لنا غرض يتعلق به في مباحث الإضافة ( فلو أردنا تخصيصه بالحقيقي قلنا ما لا مفهوم له إلا معقولاً بالقياس إلى الغير) على الوجه الذي تحققته فإن المركب مشتمل على شيء آخر كالإنسان مثلاً .
[ المقصد الثاني : خواص التكافؤ للمضاف ]
(للمضاف خواص أي خاصتان ( الأولى التكافؤ في الوجود والعدم بحسب الذهن والخارج، فكلما وجد أحدهما في الذهن أو في الخارج وجد الآخر فيه وكلما عدم أحدهما في أحدهما ( عدم ) الآخر فيه ( فإن قيل : فما قولك في المتقدم والمتأخر) بحسب الزمان فإنهما متضايفان مع أن المتقدم الزماني لا وجود له بالاعتبار الذي به كان متقدماً مع المتأخر الزماني، وكذا المتأخر لا وجود له مع وجود المتقدم؟ ( قلنا: لا وجود للحقيقي منهما إلا في (الذهن فإن التقدم والتأخر أمران اعتباريان يعتبرهما العقل إذا قاس ذات المتقدم إلى ذات المتأخر فيكون المجموع المركب منهما ومن معروضهما أيضاً اعتبارياً، فلا وجود للمضايفين هاهنا في الخارج بل في الذهن ) وهما معاً فيه ( فالتكافؤ بين الحقيقيين، وكذا بين المشهوريين المعتبرين باق بحاله ) وإما معروضاهما ) إذا أخذا وحدهما ( فقد ينفكان كالمالك والمملوك والأب والابن والمتقدم والمتأخر وليس كلامنا في ذات المعروض وحده كما نبهناك عليه. الخاصة ( الثانية : وجوب التكافؤ في النسبة ويعبر عنه أي عن التكافؤ في النسبة ( بالانعكاس) ويقال : الخاصة الثانية وجوب الانعكاس ) وهو أن يحكم بإضافة كل) من المضافين ) إلى صاحبه من حيث كان هو مضافاً إليه ) يعني أنه إذا أخذ ذات كل.
قوله : ( على الوجه الذي تحققته وهو أن يكون تعقل الغير معه من غير توقف عليه . قوله : (من حيث كان إلخ) أي من حيث كان كل واحد منها مضافا إلى صاحبه فلا وجه لإبراز الضمير.
قوله : ( فليس لنا غرض يتعلق به لعدم لزوم كونه من الأعراض لا كلاً ولا بعضاً، فلا ضير في عدم صدق التعريف عليه بناء على أن ليس من حقيقته تعقل الغير وإن كان من حقيقة عارضه ذلك .
قول : وليس كلامنا في ذات المعروض وحده كما نبهناك (عليه هذا يشعر بأن مراده بالمعروض المضاف المشهوري بالمعنى الآخر، وهو مقتضى السوق أيضاً لكن قد عرفت أنه ذات المعروض من حيث أنه معروض، فعدم الانفكاك في التعقل هاهنا أيضاً ظاهر، بل في الخارج أيضاً
The fifth observatory - the second objective: Properties of parity (al-takafu') for the relative.
I mean the composite (al-murakkab). As for the first type, i.e., the bare substrate (al-ma'rud) alone, we have no concern with it in the investigations of relation (al-idafah). If we wanted to restrict it to the real relative, we would say: that which has no concept except as conceived in relation to another, in the manner you have realized. For the composite contains something else, like man for example.
[ The second objective: Properties of parity for the relative ]
(The relative has properties, i.e., two properties. The first: parity (al-takafu') in existence and non-existence in terms of mind and external. So whenever one of them exists in the mind or in the external, the other exists therein; and whenever one of them is absent in one of them, the other is absent therein. For if it is said: What do you say about the prior (al-mutaqaddim) and the posterior (al-muta'akhkhir) in terms of time? For they are two relatives, while the temporal prior has no existence in the respect in which it is prior together with the temporal posterior, and likewise the posterior has no existence with the existence of the prior. We answer: There is no existence for the real [relative] among them except in the mind, for priority and posteriority are two relational matters that the intellect considers when it compares the essence of the prior to the essence of the posterior. Thus the composite consisting of them and their substrate is also relational. So there is no existence for the two relatives here in the external, but rather in the mind, and both of them are together in it. So parity between the two real [relatives], and likewise between the considered common [relatives], remains valid. As for their two substrates, if taken alone, they may separate, like the owner and the owned, the father and the son, and the prior and the posterior. And our discussion is not about the essence of the substrate alone, as we have reminded you. The second property: Obligatory parity in relation, and it is expressed, i.e., the parity in relation, as reciprocity (al-in'ikas). And it is said: The second property is the necessity of reciprocity. And it is that one judges that each of the two relatives is added to its partner inasmuch as it is added to it. Meaning that if the essence of each is taken...
His saying: (in the manner you have realized, which is that the conception of the other is with it without depending on it. His saying: (inasmuch as it is, etc.) i.e., inasmuch as each one of them is added to its partner, so there is no reason to manifest the pronoun.
His saying: (We have no concern with it) because it is not necessary that it be from the accidents, neither wholly nor partially. So there is no harm in the definition not applying to it, based on the fact that conceiving the other is not part of its reality, even if it is part of the reality of its accident.
His saying: And our discussion is not about the essence of the substrate alone, as we have reminded you. This indicates that what he means by the substrate is the common relative in the other sense, and this is also required by the context. However, you have known that it is the essence of the substrate insofar as it is a substrate, so non-separation in conception is also apparent here, indeed also in the external.
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage is about the two properties of al-mudaf (the relative) under the second objective: properties of parity (khawas al-takafu'). It begins by clarifying that the discussion is about the composite relative, not the bare substrate. Then it lists the two properties. The first property is parity in existence and non-existence in both mind and external reality: whenever one relative exists, the other exists; whenever one is absent, the other is absent. An objection is raised regarding temporal priority and posteriority: how can there be parity when the prior and posterior do not exist simultaneously? The answer is that the real (haqiqi) priority and posteriority exist only in the mind, as they are relational considerations; thus parity holds in the mental realm. The second property is obligatory parity in relation, called reciprocity (al-in'ikas): each relative is predicated of the other inasmuch as it is relative to it. The passage also includes commentary clarifying that the substrate alone is not the focus, and that the definition applies to the composite, not the bare substrate.

الانعكاس
المتقدم والمتأخر
المضاف الحقيقي
المضاف المشهوري
المعروض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that temporal relations like 'prior' and 'posterior' violate the parity requirement because they do not exist simultaneously in external time.
Correction: The text explains that the real (haqiqi) prior and posterior exist only in the mind as relational considerations; thus they coexist mentally, and parity holds in that realm.

Checkpoint

A) It denies that temporal priority and posteriority are true relatives.
B) It says that they exist only in the mind, so parity still holds.
C) It says that parity does not apply to temporal relations.
D) It says that the prior and posterior can exist simultaneously in the external.

Key Takeaway
Relative terms are characterized by mutual existence (parity) and mutual dependence (reciprocity); temporal relations are understood as mental constructs that preserve these properties.

Where does that relationship exist then – in external reality or only in the mind? How does this lesson challenge you to be honest about which relationships are truly mutual?

First Property: Correspondence in Existence and Non-Existence
States the first property of relatives and resolves an objection about temporal relations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first property of relative terms—correspondence in existence and non-existence—and how it applies even to temporal relations when properly understood.

Core Idea
All relatives rise and fall together: if one exists, so does the other.

Before we explore the properties of relative terms, it is essential to grasp a foundational rule: two relatives always correspond in existence and non-existence. This seems straightforward, but temporal relations like 'prior' and 'posterior' raise a puzzle. The author anticipates this objection and resolves it by clarifying the nature of such relations.

Guided Path
1
Read the property statement
Read the opening lines defining correspondence. Notice that it applies both in the mind and in the external world.
2
Consider the objection
The objection from temporal priority/posteriority challenges the property. Identify the problem: the prior does not exist when the posterior exists.
Temporal relations seem to violate correspondence.
3
Study the answer
The author distinguishes real (ḥaqīqī) relations from mental (iʿtibārī) ones. See how this saves the property.
The answer shows that such relations exist only in the mind.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
للمضاف خواص أي خاصتان ( الأولى التكافؤ في الوجود والعدم بحسب الذهن والخارج، فكلما وجد أحدهما في الذهن أو في الخارج وجد الآخر فيه وكلما عدم أحدهما في أحدهما ( عدم ) الآخر فيه ( فإن قيل : فما قولك في المتقدم والمتأخر) بحسب الزمان فإنهما متضايفان مع أن المتقدم الزماني لا وجود له بالاعتبار الذي به كان متقدماً مع المتأخر الزماني، وكذا المتأخر لا وجود له مع وجود المتقدم؟ ( قلنا: لا وجود للحقيقي منهما إلا في (الذهن فإن التقدم والتأخر أمران اعتباريان يعتبرهما العقل إذا قاس ذات المتقدم إلى ذات المتأخر فيكون المجموع المركب منهما ومن معروضهما أيضاً اعتبارياً، فلا وجود للمضايفين هاهنا في الخارج بل في الذهن ) وهما معاً فيه ( فالتكافؤ بين الحقيقيين، وكذا بين المشهوريين المعتبرين باق بحاله ) وإما معروضاهما ) إذا أخذا وحدهما ( فقد ينفكان كالمالك والمملوك والأب والابن والمتقدم والمتأخر وليس كلامنا في ذات المعروض وحده كما نبهناك عليه.
For the relative (al-mudaf) there are two properties: the first is correspondence in existence and non-existence according to the mind and external world. So whenever one of them exists in the mind or in the external, the other exists in it; and whenever one of them is absent in one of them, the other is absent in it. If it is said: What is your statement about the prior and the posterior according to time? For they are relatives while the temporal prior has no existence in the respect by which it was prior together with the temporal posterior, and likewise the posterior has no existence with the existence of the prior? We say: There is no existence of the real (haqiqi) of them except in the mind. For priority and posteriority are two relational matters (amrani i'tibariyyan) that the intellect considers when it compares the essence of the prior to the essence of the posterior. So the composite consisting of them and their substrates is also relational. Thus there is no existence for the two relatives here in the external, but in the mind, and both are in it. So the correspondence between the two real (haqiqiyayn) and likewise between the two commonly-known (mashhuriyayn) considered remains as it is. As for their two substrates, if taken alone, they may separate, like owner and owned, father and son, prior and posterior, and our speech is not about the essence of the substrate alone as we have alerted you.
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage defines and defends the first property of relative terms (mudaf): bidirectional correspondence in existence/non-existence both mentally and externally. The author states the property, then anticipates an objection from temporal priority/posteriority, where prior and posterior seem to not coexist. The answer distinguishes real existence from mental consideration: the relations of priority/posteriority are mental constructs (i'tibari), so the correspondence holds in the mind. The passage also clarifies that the property applies to the relative terms themselves, not to their independent substrates (which can separate).

تكافؤ (takafu')
وجود وعدم (wujud wa 'adam)
حقيقي (haqiqi)
مشهوري (mashhuri)
اعتباري (i'tibari)
متضايفان (mutadayifan)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Temporal relations disprove the correspondence property because the prior and posterior do not coexist in external time.
Correction: The passage explains that such relations are mental constructs, existing only in the mind, where they do coexist. The correspondence property applies to the relatives themselves, not to their temporal substrates.

Checkpoint

C) They always exist separately without relation.
D) Their correspondence is limited to the external world alone.

Key Takeaway
The first property of relatives ensures that two relatives always coexist in existence and non-existence, and apparent counterexamples like temporal relations are resolved by recognizing that they exist only as mental constructs.

Second Property of Relative Terms: Inversion (al-In'ikas)
Defines inversion as the second property of relative terms with condition of aspect and distinction from real relatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second property of relative terms—the necessity of inversion—and its dependency on the aspect of relation and the distinction between mashhuri and haqiqi relatives.

Core Idea
Inversion of relative terms holds only when the aspect of relation is preserved.

This passage introduces the second property of relative terms: the necessity of correspondence in relation, called inversion (al-in'ikas). It explains that inversion is required only when each relative is considered under the specific aspect of being relative to the other. Without this aspect, inversion fails, and the property applies only to mashhuri (commonly-known) relatives, not to real (haqiqi) relatives like pure relations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Get a sense of the author's flow and technical terms.
Notice the terms 'haythiyya' and 'mashhuri' vs 'haqiqi'.
2
Identify the condition for inversion
Find where the author states that inversion is necessary only when the aspect (haythiyya) of being relative is taken. Note the example of father and son.
The passage explicitly says 'because if this aspect is not observed, inversion is not necessary.'
3
Distinguish mashhuri from haqiqi relatives
See how the author limits inversion to mashhuri relatives (substrate-based, like father) and excludes haqiqi relatives (pure relations, like fatherhood). The qayd of haythiyya signals this.
This distinction is crucial for applying the property correctly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخاصة ( الثانية : وجوب التكافؤ في النسبة ويعبر عنه أي عن التكافؤ في النسبة ( بالانعكاس) ويقال : الخاصة الثانية وجوب الانعكاس ) وهو أن يحكم بإضافة كل) من المضافين ) إلى صاحبه من حيث كان هو مضافاً إليه ) يعني أنه إذا أخذ ذات كل واحد من المضافين من حيث أنه مضايف لصاحبه ونسب أحدهما إلى الآخر وجب أن تنعكس هذه النسبة فينتسب الآخر إليه أيضاً ( فكما أن الأب أبو الابن فالابن ابن الأب وإنما اعتبرنا الحيثية وقلنا : من حيث كان مضافاً إليه ( لأنه ) إذا لم يراع هذه الحيثية (لم يجب الانعكاس فإنك إذا قلت هذا أب لإنسان لم يلزم أن هذا إنسان لأب والحاصل أن هذه الخاصة إنما هي للمضاف المشهوري أعني المعروض المأخوذ من حيث أنه معروض لعارضه كالأب والابن والعالم والمعلوم والعاشق والمعشوق، حتى إذا نسب أحد المشهوريين إلى صاحبه وجب انعكاس هذه النسبة، وأما المضاف الحقيقي فلا نسبة فيه حتى يتصور الانعكاس إذا لا معنى لقولك الأبوة أبوة البنوة، وفي قيد الحيثية إشارة إلى ذلك لمن كان له قلب فتذكر
The second property: the necessity of correspondence in relation, and it is expressed — i.e., correspondence in relation — as inversion (al-in'ikas). And it is said: the second property is the necessity of inversion. It is that one judges the relation of each of the two relatives to its companion from the aspect (haythiyya) that it is relative to it, meaning that when one takes the essence of each of the two relatives from the aspect that it is relative (mudyaf) to its companion and relates one to the other, it is necessary that this relation be inverted so that the other also relates to it. For just as the father (al-ab) is father of the son (al-ibn), so the son is son of the father. We only considered the aspect and said 'from the aspect that it is relative to it' because if this aspect is not observed, inversion is not necessary. For if you say 'this is a father of a human,' it does not follow that 'this human is a father.' The conclusion is that this property is only for the commonly-known (mashhuri) relative, i.e., the substrate (ma'rud) taken from the aspect that it is a substrate for its accident ('arid), such as father and son, knower and known, lover and beloved. So when one of the two mashhuriyin is related to its companion, the inversion of this relation is necessary. As for the real (haqiqi) relative, there is no relation in it such that inversion can be conceived, for there is no meaning to saying 'fatherhood is fatherhood of sonship.' In the restriction (qayd) of the aspect there is an indication of this for anyone who has a heart to remember.
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage defines the second property of relative terms: the necessity of correspondence in relation, called inversion (al-in'ikas). It requires that when you take each relative under a specific aspect (haythiyya) — namely, as being relative to the other — the relation must invert. The example: father is father of son, son is son of father. If you drop the aspect (e.g., say 'father of a human'), inversion fails. The property applies only to mashhuri (commonly-known) relatives, which have a substrate (like father as a person), not to haqiqi (real) relatives which are pure relations (like fatherhood). The restriction to the aspect signals this distinction.

Key Terms
انعكاس (in'ikas)
نسبة (nisba)
حيثية (haythiyya)
مضاف مشهوري (mudaf mashhuri)
مضاف حقيقي (mudaf haqiqi)
معروض (ma'rud)
عارض ('arid)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Inversion applies to all relative terms regardless of aspect or type.
Correction: Inversion only holds when the aspect (haythiyya) of being relative is maintained, and only for mashhuri relatives, not for haqiqi relatives.

Checkpoint
Why is inversion not necessary if you say 'this is a father of a human' instead of 'this is a father of this son'?
A) Because 'human' is not a relative term.
B) Because the aspect (haythiyya) of being relative is not specified.
C) Because inversion only applies to real (haqiqi) relatives.
D) Because the term 'father' is ambiguous.

Key Takeaway
Inversion of relative terms is necessary only when the relative is considered under the aspect of being relative to its companion, and it applies to mashhuri relatives, not haqiqi.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider your relationship with Allah as 'servant' ('abd). Are you relating to Him from the aspect of being a servant? If you changed that aspect and considered, say, your own desires, would the inversion of love and obedience still hold? What does this lesson teach you about the conditions of your relationship with your Creator?
?

How does recognizing that inversion requires a specific aspect change the way you fulfill your duties in that relationship? Can you identify a time you assumed inversion without preserving the aspect, and what was the result?

Difficulty of Inversion When One Side Lacks a Name
Explains difficulty of inversion when one relative lacks a name and presents the solution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why inversion can be difficult when one relative lacks a distinct name and how to resolve it using compound relational expressions.

Core Idea
Inversion may be impossible with simple terms but can be achieved by coining a relational expression.

This passage from al-Sanusi addresses a practical problem in applying the rule of inversion: what happens when one of the two relatives has no dedicated name? For example, 'wing' relates to 'bird' but there is no simple term for the bird considered as the possessor of a wing. The text explains how to overcome this difficulty.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on difficulty of inversion
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the problem introduced in the first sentence.
2
Understand the 'wing' example
The text uses 'wing' (janah) as an example. Why can we say 'wing of bird' but not 'bird of wing'? Identify which side lacks a relational name.
The bird side lacks a term like 'winged'.
3
See the solution
The solution is to coin a phrase like 'dhu al-janah' (possessor of a wing). This creates a relational expression that allows inversion.
4
Note the necessity
With this compound expression, inversion becomes necessary. Understand why the relation is now symmetric.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد تصعب رعاية قاعدة الانعكاس سيما إذا لم يكن له أي للمضاف ( من الجانب الآخر اسم كالجناح) فإنه اسم لأحد المتضايفين مأخوذاً مع إضافته، وليس للمضاف الآخر أعني الطير اسم كذلك فيقال : الجناح جناح الطير ولا يقال : الطير طير الجناح وإن شئت رعاية قاعدة الانعكاس هاهنا ( فاعتبره ) أي المضاف ( من الطرف الآخر بلفظ دال على النسبة كذي الجناح فإنه يجب الانعكاس حينئذ والضابط في معرفة طريق الانعكاس أن تجمع أوصاف كل واحد من الطرفين وتنظر فيها، فأي وصف وجدته بحيث إذا وضعته ورفعت ما عداه بقيت الإضافة بينهما ، وإذا رفعته ووضعت غيره مكانه
And the observance of the rule of inversion may become difficult, especially if there is no name for the relative from the other side, like 'wing' (al-janah). For it is a name for one of the two relatives taken with its relation, and there is no name for the other relative, i.e., the bird, like that. So it is said: 'the wing is the wing of the bird' and it is not said 'the bird is the bird of the wing.' But if you wish to observe the rule of inversion here, consider the relative from the other side with a word indicating relation, like 'dhu al-janah' (the one with the wing), for then inversion becomes necessary. And the general rule (al-dabit) for knowing the method of inversion is that you gather the descriptions of each of the two sides and look into them. Whichever description you find such that if you posit it and remove what is other than it, the relation remains between them, and if you remove it and posit something else in its place, [the relation does not remain].
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage addresses a practical difficulty in applying inversion: when one relative lacks a distinct name, like 'wing' relative to 'bird.' You can say 'wing of bird' but not 'bird of wing.' The solution is to coin a relational expression from the other side, like 'dhu al-janah' (the one with the wing) to allow inversion. Then the passage gives a general rule (al-dabit) for identifying the correct inversion relationship: examine the descriptions of both sides; find the description that, when retained and others removed, preserves the relation between them. This test isolates the essential relational attribute.

The General Rule for Identifying Inversion
Presents the general rule for determining the correct inversion relationship.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the general rule for finding the essential relational description that permits inversion.

Core Idea
Gather all descriptions of both sides and test which one preserves the relation when others are removed.

After solving the naming difficulty, the text provides a universal method for identifying the correct inversion relationship. This rule is a logical test that isolates the essential attribute linking two relatives.

Guided Path
1
Read the general rule passage
Read the Arabic and translation, focusing on the procedural nature of the rule.
2
Understand the test
The rule says: gather all descriptions of each side. Then test each description by positing it and removing all others. If the relation remains, that description is essential.
3
Apply to an example
Try the rule with a simple example like 'father' and 'son'. The descriptions might include 'parent', 'male', etc. Which one is essential?
The essential description is 'parent' because it alone preserves the relation.
4
Reflect on the purpose
This rule prevents false inversions by ensuring the correct attribute is used.

The Reciprocal Property and the Condition of Aspect
Sanusi states the rule of reciprocity with the aspect condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the basic rule of reciprocity for relative terms and why the aspect (haythiyya) is necessary for it to hold.

Core Idea
Reciprocity only applies when terms are taken precisely as relatives.

Sanusi begins by stating a fundamental property: when one relative is considered strictly in its relational aspect, the relation must be reciprocal. However, he immediately warns that this only works if we respect the condition of 'insofar as'. This lesson unpacks that condition and why it matters.

Guided Path
1
Read the basic rule
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Notice how Sanusi introduces the property: if one relative is taken 'insofar as it is related' to its partner, then the relation must reflect back.
Focus on the phrase 'min haythu kana mudaafan'.
2
Identify the condition
The author emphasizes the word 'insofar as' (min haythu). Think about why this condition is necessary. Without it, the rule fails, as shown in the father/human counterexample.
This condition distinguishes true reciprocal relatives from accidental relations.
3
Distinguish the two types
The passage introduces two types: the well-known relative (al-mudaf al-mashhuri) and the true relative (al-mudaf al-haqiqi). Note how only the former has a substrate that can be taken with the relation.
Examples: father/son, knower/known are well-known relatives; fatherhood itself is a true relative.

المرصد الخامس - المقصد الثاني : خواص التكافؤ للمضاف
واحد من المضافين من حيث أنه مضايف لصاحبه ونسب أحدهما إلى الآخر وجب أن تنعكس هذه النسبة فينتسب الآخر إليه أيضاً ( فكما أن الأب أبو الابن فالابن ابن الأب وإنما اعتبرنا الحيثية وقلنا : من حيث كان مضافاً إليه ( لأنه ) إذا لم يراع هذه الحيثية (لم يجب الانعكاس فإنك إذا قلت هذا أب لإنسان لم يلزم أن هذا إنسان لأب والحاصل أن هذه الخاصة إنما هي للمضاف المشهوري أعني المعروض المأخوذ من حيث أنه معروض لعارضه كالأب والابن والعالم والمعلوم والعاشق والمعشوق، حتى إذا نسب أحد المشهوريين إلى صاحبه وجب انعكاس هذه النسبة، وأما المضاف الحقيقي فلا نسبة فيه حتى يتصور الانعكاس إذا لا معنى لقولك الأبوة أبوة البنوة، وفي قيد الحيثية إشارة إلى ذلك لمن كان له قلب فتذكر ( وقد تصعب رعاية قاعدة الانعكاس سيما إذا لم يكن له أي للمضاف ( من الجانب الآخر اسم كالجناح) فإنه اسم لأحد المتضايفين مأخوذاً مع إضافته، وليس للمضاف الآخر أعني الطير اسم كذلك فيقال : الجناح جناح الطير ولا يقال : الطير طير الجناح وإن شئت رعاية قاعدة الانعكاس هاهنا ( فاعتبره ) أي المضاف ( من الطرف الآخر بلفظ دال على النسبة كذي الجناح فإنه يجب الانعكاس حينئذ والضابط في معرفة طريق الانعكاس أن تجمع أوصاف كل واحد من الطرفين وتنظر فيها، فأي وصف وجدته بحيث إذا وضعته ورفعت ما عداه بقيت الإضافة بينهما ، وإذا رفعته ووضعت غيره مكانه
قوله : إشارة إلى ذلك لأن قيد الحيثية يشعر بأن فيه حيثية أخرى سوى كونه مضافاً إلى صاحبه، وذلك المضاف المشهوري ذات الموصوف بخلاف الحقيقي فإنه لا ماهية له سوى الإضافة لأنه النسبة المتكررة.
إذا كان مما له وجود فيه، اللهم إلا أن يراد بالمعروض هاهنا ذاته من حيث هي والتنبيه على ما ذكره فيما سبق باعتبار أن العرض إذا لم يتعلق بالمعروض من حيث هو معروض، فعدم تعلقه به من حيث ذاته بالطريق الأولى فتأمل.
قوله: (وفي قيد الحيثية إشارة إلى ذلك أي إلى الحاصل المذكور ووجه الإشارة أن في قيد الحيثية إشارة إلى أن في كل من المضافين اللذين حكم بوجوب انعكاس النسبة بينما جهة يئية الإضافة والإلغاء هذا القيد وهذا إنما يظهر في المضاف المشهوري فإن فيه ذات المضاف وحقيقة الإضافة، وأما المضاف الحقيقي فلا شيء فيه غير جهة الإضافة إذ لا حقيقة لها سوى أنها نسبة معقولة بالقياس إلى الغير المخصوص .
قوله : ( بحيث إذا وضعته ورفعت (ما عداه مثلاً إذا رفعت من الابن كونه حيواناً أو إنساناً أو ما شئت من الأوصاف وأثبت كونه ابناً بقيت إضافة الأب، وإن رفعت عن كونه ابناً وأثبت له سائر أوصاف لم يبق الإضافة، فعلمنا أن الإضافة الحقيقية الواجبة الانعكاس إنما هي بين الأب والابن،
لم تبق تلك الإضافة، فذلك الوصف هو الإضافة الحقيقة فإذا عبرت عن كل واحد من الطرفين بما يدل عليه مأخوذاً مع الإضافة الحقيقة سواء كان لفظاً مفرداً أو مركباً ونسبت أحدهما إلى الآخر انعكست تلك النسبة قطعاً .
The Fifth Observatory - Second Objective: Properties of Equivalence for the Relative Term. One of the two relatives, insofar as it is related to its associate and one of them is related to the other, this relation must be reflected so that the other is also related to it. For just as the father is father of the son, so the son is son of the father. We only considered the aspect (al-haythiyya) and said: 'insofar as it is related to it' because if this aspect is not observed, the reflection is not necessary. For if you say 'this is a father to a human', it does not necessarily follow that this human is a father. The conclusion is that this property is only for the well-known relative (al-mudaf al-mashhuri), meaning the substrate (al-ma'rud) taken insofar as it is a substrate for its accident, like father and son, knower and known, lover and beloved. So when one of the two well-known relatives is related to its associate, the reflection of this relation is necessary. As for the true relative (al-mudaf al-haqiqi), there is no relation in it for reflection to be conceivable, for there is no meaning to your saying 'fatherhood is fatherhood of sonship'. In the condition of the aspect there is an indication of that for whoever has a heart to remember. And the observance of the rule of reflection may be difficult, especially when there is no name for the relative from the other side, like 'wing' (al-janah), for it is a name for one of the two relatives taken with its relation, and the other relative—namely the bird—has no such name. So it is said 'the wing is a wing of the bird' and not 'the bird is a bird of the wing'. If you wish to observe the rule of reflection here, consider it (i.e., the relative) from the other side with a term indicating the relation, like 'the one with the wing' (dhu al-janah), for then reflection is necessary. The rule for knowing the way of reflection is to gather the attributes of each of the two sides and examine them. Any attribute you find such that if you posit it and remove what is other than it, the relation between them remains, and if you remove it and posit something else in its place, the relation does not remain—that attribute is the true relation. So if you express each of the two sides by what indicates it taken with the true relation, whether it is a single word or a compound, and you relate one to the other, that relation is certainly reflected. His saying: 'Indication of that' because the condition of the aspect indicates that there is another aspect besides its being related to its associate, and that is the well-known relative, which is the essence of the described, unlike the true relative which has no essence other than the relation because it is the repeated relation. If it has existence in it, unless what is meant by the substrate here is its essence as such—and attention to what he mentioned previously considering that if the accident is not attached to the substrate insofar as it is a substrate, then its non-attachment to it in terms of its essence is all the more so, so reflect. His saying: 'And in the condition of the aspect there is an indication of that' i.e., to the mentioned conclusion, and the aspect of the indication is that in the condition of the aspect there is an indication that in each of the two relatives for which the necessity of reflection of the relation is judged, there is a relational aspect and its cancellation—this condition, and this appears only in the well-known relative, for in it there is the essence of the relative and the reality of the relation. As for the true relative, there is nothing in it other than the relational aspect, for it has no reality other than that it is a relation conceived in relation to the specific other. His saying: 'Such that if you posit it and remove what is other than it'—for example, if you remove from the son its being animal or human or any attributes you wish and affirm its being a son, the relation of the father remains; but if you remove from it its being a son and affirm for it the rest of the attributes, the relation does not remain. So we know that the true relation which necessitates reflection is between father and son. That relation does not remain, so that attribute is the true relation. So if you express each of the two sides by what indicates it taken with the true relation, whether it is a single word or a compound, and you relate one to the other, that relation is certainly reflected.
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage is a detailed explanation of the property of reciprocity (al-in'ikas) for relative terms (al-mudaf), specifically for what the author calls 'the well-known relative' (al-mudaf al-mashhuri). The discussion begins by stating the basic rule: when one of two relatives is considered precisely insofar as it is related to the other (min haythu kana mudaafan li sahibihi), the relation must be reciprocal. The author immediately qualifies this by emphasizing the crucial phrase 'insofar as' (min haythu). Without this qualification, reciprocity does not hold: for example, 'this is a father to a human' does not imply 'this human is a father' because 'human' is not taken in its relational aspect. The true property belongs only to the well-known relative, which is a concrete entity (like a person) taken as a substrate (ma'rud) for an accidental relation (like fatherhood). Examples include father/son, knower/known, lover/beloved. In such cases, if one is explicitly related to the other as a relative, the relation must be reciprocal. In contrast, the true relative (al-mudaf al-haqiqi) is the relation itself (e.g., fatherhood), which has no independent essence but is merely the relation between two things. For the true relative, reciprocity is not conceived because there is no relation between two independent terms. The author then addresses a practical difficulty: when one side lacks a specific relational term (e.g., 'wing' is a term for one side but 'bird' does not have a reciprocal term 'birdness' for the wing). The solution is to use a phrase that indicates the relation from the other side, such as 'the one with the wing' (dhu al-janah). Finally, the author provides a general rule for identifying the essential relation that must be reciprocal: collect all attributes of both sides; if an attribute, when kept while others are removed, preserves the relation between them, and when removed, the relation disappears, then that attribute is the true relation. Expressing each side by that attribute (with the relation) guarantees reciprocity. The commentary following the main text further clarifies the distinction between the well-known and true relative, emphasizing that the condition of the aspect signals the presence of a substrate beyond mere relation.

Key Terms
مضاف مشهوري
مضاف حقيقي
انعكاس
حيثية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that any relation between two things is automatically reciprocal.
Correction: Reciprocity only applies when the terms are taken precisely as relatives (with the aspect condition). For example, 'father' and 'son' are reciprocal, but 'father' and 'human' are not.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the statement 'this is a father to a human' not imply 'this human is a father'?
A) Because 'human' is not a relational term.
B) Because the aspect (haythiyya) of being related is not observed for 'human'.
C) Because the relation is not reciprocal for any relative term.
D) Because 'father' is not a well-known relative.

Key Takeaway
Reciprocity of relations only holds when the terms are taken strictly in their relational aspect, and this applies only to well-known relatives (concrete substrates), not to the relations themselves.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider a relationship in your life where you expect reciprocity (e.g., parent-child, teacher-student). According to the aspect condition, when is that expectation valid? When does it break down? How does this lesson change how you view that relationship?
?
The lesson distinguishes between the concrete entity (mudaf mashhuri) and the relation itself (mudaf haqiqi). When it comes to your faith, do you sometimes treat your relationship with Allah as if it were a true relative (a mere concept) rather than a real, concrete bond? How can you shift your mindset to the well-known relative?
?
Reflect on a time you misunderstood someone because you assumed reciprocity without considering the aspect. How can you apply the aspect condition to avoid such misunderstandings in the future?

Practical Difficulty: When One Side Lacks a Term
Sanusi gives an example of a missing term and provides a solution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how to handle cases where one relative lacks a corresponding term, such as 'wing' and 'bird'.

Core Idea
Use a descriptive phrase like 'the one with the wing' to achieve reciprocity.

Sanusi now addresses a practical challenge: sometimes only one side of a relation has a specific name (e.g., 'wing' for the wing, but no 'wingness' for the bird). He shows how we can still preserve reciprocity by using a term that indicates the relation from the other side.

Guided Path
1
Read the wing example
Read the Arabic text about the wing and bird. Note that 'wing' is a term for one relative, but there is no equivalent term for the bird from the wing's perspective.
The example illustrates a gap in relational vocabulary.
2
Understand the problem
Why can't we say 'the bird is a bird of the wing'? Because 'bird' is not taken as a relational term for the wing; it's a thing that has a wing.
The rule of reciprocity requires both sides to be expressed as relatives.
3
Learn the solution
Sanusi suggests using a phrase like 'the one with the wing' (dhu al-janah) for the bird, which indicates the relation. Then reciprocity works: the wing is the wing of the one with the wing, and the one with the wing is the one with the wing of the wing.

المرصد الخامس - المقصد الثاني : خواص التكافؤ للمضاف
واحد من المضافين من حيث أنه مضايف لصاحبه ونسب أحدهما إلى الآخر وجب أن تنعكس هذه النسبة فينتسب الآخر إليه أيضاً ( فكما أن الأب أبو الابن فالابن ابن الأب وإنما اعتبرنا الحيثية وقلنا : من حيث كان مضافاً إليه ( لأنه ) إذا لم يراع هذه الحيثية (لم يجب الانعكاس فإنك إذا قلت هذا أب لإنسان لم يلزم أن هذا إنسان لأب والحاصل أن هذه الخاصة إنما هي للمضاف المشهوري أعني المعروض المأخوذ من حيث أنه معروض لعارضه كالأب والابن والعالم والمعلوم والعاشق والمعشوق، حتى إذا نسب أحد المشهوريين إلى صاحبه وجب انعكاس هذه النسبة، وأما المضاف الحقيقي فلا نسبة فيه حتى يتصور الانعكاس إذا لا معنى لقولك الأبوة أبوة البنوة، وفي قيد الحيثية إشارة إلى ذلك لمن كان له قلب فتذكر ( وقد تصعب رعاية قاعدة الانعكاس سيما إذا لم يكن له أي للمضاف ( من الجانب الآخر اسم كالجناح) فإنه اسم لأحد المتضايفين مأخوذاً مع إضافته، وليس للمضاف الآخر أعني الطير اسم كذلك فيقال : الجناح جناح الطير ولا يقال : الطير طير الجناح وإن شئت رعاية قاعدة الانعكاس هاهنا ( فاعتبره ) أي المضاف ( من الطرف الآخر بلفظ دال على النسبة كذي الجناح فإنه يجب الانعكاس حينئذ والضابط في معرفة طريق الانعكاس أن تجمع أوصاف كل واحد من الطرفين وتنظر فيها، فأي وصف وجدته بحيث إذا وضعته ورفعت ما عداه بقيت الإضافة بينهما ، وإذا رفعته ووضعت غيره مكانه
قوله : إشارة إلى ذلك لأن قيد الحيثية يشعر بأن فيه حيثية أخرى سوى كونه مضافاً إلى صاحبه، وذلك المضاف المشهوري ذات الموصوف بخلاف الحقيقي فإنه لا ماهية له سوى الإضافة لأنه النسبة المتكررة.
إذا كان مما له وجود فيه، اللهم إلا أن يراد بالمعروض هاهنا ذاته من حيث هي والتنبيه على ما ذكره فيما سبق باعتبار أن العرض إذا لم يتعلق بالمعروض من حيث هو معروض، فعدم تعلقه به من حيث ذاته بالطريق الأولى فتأمل.
قوله: (وفي قيد الحيثية إشارة إلى ذلك أي إلى الحاصل المذكور ووجه الإشارة أن في قيد الحيثية إشارة إلى أن في كل من المضافين اللذين حكم بوجوب انعكاس النسبة بينما جهة يئية الإضافة والإلغاء هذا القيد وهذا إنما يظهر في المضاف المشهوري فإن فيه ذات المضاف وحقيقة الإضافة، وأما المضاف الحقيقي فلا شيء فيه غير جهة الإضافة إذ لا حقيقة لها سوى أنها نسبة معقولة بالقياس إلى الغير المخصوص .
قوله : ( بحيث إذا وضعته ورفعت (ما عداه مثلاً إذا رفعت من الابن كونه حيواناً أو إنساناً أو ما شئت من الأوصاف وأثبت كونه ابناً بقيت إضافة الأب، وإن رفعت عن كونه ابناً وأثبت له سائر أوصاف لم يبق الإضافة، فعلمنا أن الإضافة الحقيقية الواجبة الانعكاس إنما هي بين الأب والابن،
لم تبق تلك الإضافة، فذلك الوصف هو الإضافة الحقيقة فإذا عبرت عن كل واحد من الطرفين بما يدل عليه مأخوذاً مع الإضافة الحقيقة سواء كان لفظاً مفرداً أو مركباً ونسبت أحدهما إلى الآخر انعكست تلك النسبة قطعاً .
The Fifth Observatory - Second Objective: Properties of Equivalence for the Relative Term. One of the two relatives, insofar as it is related to its associate and one of them is related to the other, this relation must be reflected so that the other is also related to it. For just as the father is father of the son, so the son is son of the father. We only considered the aspect (al-haythiyya) and said: 'insofar as it is related to it' because if this aspect is not observed, the reflection is not necessary. For if you say 'this is a father to a human', it does not necessarily follow that this human is a father. The conclusion is that this property is only for the well-known relative (al-mudaf al-mashhuri), meaning the substrate (al-ma'rud) taken insofar as it is a substrate for its accident, like father and son, knower and known, lover and beloved. So when one of the two well-known relatives is related to its associate, the reflection of this relation is necessary. As for the true relative (al-mudaf al-haqiqi), there is no relation in it for reflection to be conceivable, for there is no meaning to your saying 'fatherhood is fatherhood of sonship'. In the condition of the aspect there is an indication of that for whoever has a heart to remember. And the observance of the rule of reflection may be difficult, especially when there is no name for the relative from the other side, like 'wing' (al-janah), for it is a name for one of the two relatives taken with its relation, and the other relative—namely the bird—has no such name. So it is said 'the wing is a wing of the bird' and not 'the bird is a bird of the wing'. If you wish to observe the rule of reflection here, consider it (i.e., the relative) from the other side with a term indicating the relation, like 'the one with the wing' (dhu al-janah), for then reflection is necessary. The rule for knowing the way of reflection is to gather the attributes of each of the two sides and examine them. Any attribute you find such that if you posit it and remove what is other than it, the relation between them remains, and if you remove it and posit something else in its place, the relation does not remain—that attribute is the true relation. So if you express each of the two sides by what indicates it taken with the true relation, whether it is a single word or a compound, and you relate one to the other, that relation is certainly reflected. His saying: 'Indication of that' because the condition of the aspect indicates that there is another aspect besides its being related to its associate, and that is the well-known relative, which is the essence of the described, unlike the true relative which has no essence other than the relation because it is the repeated relation. If it has existence in it, unless what is meant by the substrate here is its essence as such—and attention to what he mentioned previously considering that if the accident is not attached to the substrate insofar as it is a substrate, then its non-attachment to it in terms of its essence is all the more so, so reflect. His saying: 'And in the condition of the aspect there is an indication of that' i.e., to the mentioned conclusion, and the aspect of the indication is that in the condition of the aspect there is an indication that in each of the two relatives for which the necessity of reflection of the relation is judged, there is a relational aspect and its cancellation—this condition, and this appears only in the well-known relative, for in it there is the essence of the relative and the reality of the relation. As for the true relative, there is nothing in it other than the relational aspect, for it has no reality other than that it is a relation conceived in relation to the specific other. His saying: 'Such that if you posit it and remove what is other than it'—for example, if you remove from the son its being animal or human or any attributes you wish and affirm its being a son, the relation of the father remains; but if you remove from it its being a son and affirm for it the rest of the attributes, the relation does not remain. So we know that the true relation which necessitates reflection is between father and son. That relation does not remain, so that attribute is the true relation. So if you express each of the two sides by what indicates it taken with the true relation, whether it is a single word or a compound, and you relate one to the other, that relation is certainly reflected.
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage is a detailed explanation of the property of reciprocity (al-in'ikas) for relative terms (al-mudaf), specifically for what the author calls 'the well-known relative' (al-mudaf al-mashhuri). The discussion begins by stating the basic rule: when one of two relatives is considered precisely insofar as it is related to the other (min haythu kana mudaafan li sahibihi), the relation must be reciprocal. The author immediately qualifies this by emphasizing the crucial phrase 'insofar as' (min haythu). Without this qualification, reciprocity does not hold: for example, 'this is a father to a human' does not imply 'this human is a father' because 'human' is not taken in its relational aspect. The true property belongs only to the well-known relative, which is a concrete entity (like a person) taken as a substrate (ma'rud) for an accidental relation (like fatherhood). Examples include father/son, knower/known, lover/beloved. In such cases, if one is explicitly related to the other as a relative, the relation must be reciprocal. In contrast, the true relative (al-mudaf al-haqiqi) is the relation itself (e.g., fatherhood), which has no independent essence but is merely the relation between two things. For the true relative, reciprocity is not conceived because there is no relation between two independent terms. The author then addresses a practical difficulty: when one side lacks a specific relational term (e.g., 'wing' is a term for one side but 'bird' does not have a reciprocal term 'birdness' for the wing). The solution is to use a phrase that indicates the relation from the other side, such as 'the one with the wing' (dhu al-janah). Finally, the author provides a general rule for identifying the essential relation that must be reciprocal: collect all attributes of both sides; if an attribute, when kept while others are removed, preserves the relation between them, and when removed, the relation disappears, then that attribute is the true relation. Expressing each side by that attribute (with the relation) guarantees reciprocity. The commentary following the main text further clarifies the distinction between the well-known and true relative, emphasizing that the condition of the aspect signals the presence of a substrate beyond mere relation.

Key Terms
جناح
ذو الجناح
انعكاس

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how can we achieve reciprocity in the case of 'wing' and 'bird'?
A) By using the term 'bird' as a relative.
B) By using a phrase like 'the one with the wing' for the bird.
C) By accepting that reciprocity does not apply.
D) By inventing a new word for the bird's relation.

Key Takeaway
When one side lacks a relational term, we can still achieve reciprocity by using a descriptive phrase that indicates the relation from that side.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself unable to articulate your relationship with your Creator because the right words escape you, will you settle for silence—or will you craft a descriptive phrase, as Sanusi did, to restore the connection? What phrase might you use today?
?
In your daily life, do you ever assume that because something lacks a label, it is less real or less reciprocal? Can you think of a relationship or duty where you have neglected its 'reflection' simply because you lacked the term? How can you reframe it now?
?
If the rule of reflection teaches that every relation demands a reciprocal description, what does that demand of you in your relationship with the Quran? Are you content with one-sided knowledge, or will you actively seek the descriptive phrase that makes your faith reciprocal?

Logic and Technical Terms
intermediate
7 min
The Rule for Identifying the True Relation
Sanusi provides a method to identify the true relational attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the method to identify the essential relation that guarantees reciprocity.

Core Idea
The true relation is the attribute whose preservation maintains the relation.

Sanusi concludes with a general rule: to find the relation that must be reciprocal, collect all attributes of both sides and test them. The attribute that, when kept, preserves the relation, and when removed, destroys it, is the true relation. This lesson unpacks that test.

Guided Path
1
Read the rule passage
Read the Arabic text starting from 'The rule for knowing the way of reflection...' (والضابط). Understand the method: gather all attributes of both sides.
The method is an empirical test.
2
Understand the test
For each attribute, check if by keeping it and removing others, the relation remains. If yes, that attribute is the true relation. If removing it breaks the relation, it is essential.
Example: for father and son, being a son is the essential attribute.
3
Apply the rule
Once you identify the true relation, express each side with that attribute (including relation) and reciprocity will hold.
This ensures you have the correct reciprocal terms.

Commentary
This passage is a detailed explanation of the property of reciprocity (al-in'ikas) for relative terms (al-mudaf), specifically for what the author calls 'the well-known relative' (al-mudaf al-mashhuri). Without this qualification, reciprocity does not hold: for example, 'this is a father to a human' does not imply 'this human is a father' because 'human' is not taken in its relational aspect. The true property belongs only to the well-known relative, which is a concrete entity (like a person) taken as a substrate (ma'rud) for an accidental relation (like fatherhood). Examples include father/son, knower/known, lover/beloved. In such cases, if one is explicitly related to the other as a relative, the relation must be reciprocal. In contrast, the true relative (al-mudaf al-haqiqi) is the relation itself (e.g., fatherhood), which has no independent essence but is merely the relation between two things. For the true relative, reciprocity is not conceived because there is no relation between two independent terms. The author then addresses a practical difficulty: when one side lacks a specific relational term (e.g., 'wing' is a term for one side but 'bird' does not have a reciprocal term 'birdness' for the wing). The solution is to use a phrase that indicates the relation from the other side, such as 'the one with the wing' (dhu al-janah). Expressing each side by that attribute (with the relation) guarantees reciprocity. The commentary following the main text further clarifies the distinction between the well-known and true relative, emphasizing that the condition of the aspect signals the presence of a substrate beyond mere relation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any attribute common to both relatives can serve as the basis for reciprocity.
Correction: Only the specific attribute that, when kept, maintains the relation, and when removed, destroys it, is the true relation. For example, 'son' not 'human'.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how do you identify the true relation between two relatives?
A) By choosing the most common attribute.
B) By testing which attribute, when kept alone, preserves the relation.
C) By taking the first attribute that comes to mind.
D) By using the attribute that has a specific term.

Key Takeaway
The true relation is identified by a test: it is the attribute whose presence ensures the relation and whose absence breaks it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a relationship in your life that is strained or distant. What attributes of that relationship have you been focusing on? Using Sanusi's rule, what is the true relation that must be preserved for that relationship to flourish? Will you commit to prioritizing that true relation starting today?
?
Consider your relationship with Allah. What attributes of yourself do you focus on—your deeds, your knowledge, your status? According to the lesson, the true relation between you and your Creator is that you are His servant (ʿabd) and He is your Lord (Rabb). Are you living in a way that preserves this relation, or are you distracted by other attributes? What will you change after this lesson?
?
The lesson teaches that the true relation is the one whose absence breaks the connection. In your daily life, what non-essential attributes (e.g., wealth, popularity, achievements) are you clinging to, thinking they define you? If those were removed, would your relationship with Allah, family, or community still stand? Write down one specific step you will take to focus on the true relation in one key area of your life.

The Nature of Relation as Dependent on Things
Sharh defines relation (al-idafah) as non-independent and explains its two modes of conceptualization.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why relation is not an independent category and how it is realized through its attachment to things.

Core Idea
Relation has no essence of its own; its entire reality is being attached to something else.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif delves into the category of relation (al-idafah). Unlike other categories that have some independent essence, relation is purely relational—it cannot be conceived without reference to its subject. The author explains two ways to understand how relation achieves its specification, and clarifies a common misconception about its non-independence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get familiar with the main claims about relation. Notice how the author stresses that relation does not subsist independently.
Pay attention to the two modes of conceptualization mentioned.
2
Identify the two modes of conceptualization
The passage presents two ways to understand relation's dependence: (1) by taking the attached thing and relation together, and (2) by taking relation accompanied by the specific attachment as a restricted thing. Compare these two modes.
3
Understand the clarification of 'non-independent'
The commentary clarifies that 'non-independent' does not mean lack of external existence (since all accidents are dependent), but that the essence of relation cannot be conceived without the subject.
This is a common philosophical nuance.
4
Connect to broader kalam context
Reflect on how this definition of relation differs from other categories. The passage notes that other categories are states (hay'ah) that give rise to relation, while relation itself is the relation.
This distinction is important in discussions of divine attributes.

[Third Objective: Relation and its Dependence on Things] Relation does not subsist independently of its existence; i.e., it does not have an isolated existence such that its determination could be conceived by itself, but its existence is that it is something adhering to things. So its realization and specification are dependent on the realization of its attachment to the other and its specification. And that is understood—i.e., its realization is dependent on attachment—sometimes by taking the attached thing and the relation together, so the relation is determined according to the determination of the attached thing and the attachment. And that taken in this way is not the category (al-maqulah), but rather it is something composed of the category and its substrate. And sometimes by taking the relation accompanied by the specific attachment as one restricted thing, which attaches to that attached thing. And this is the diversification and realization of relation: similarity (al-mushabahah) is union (al-ittihad). His saying: 'i.e., it does not have an isolated existence, etc.' — The intended meaning is not what is imagined from its apparent sense, that it does not have an existence in the outside isolated from the subject, because all accidents are like that, and because external existence has no role in the realization of the essence. Rather, the meaning is that it does not have a realization in itself and its essence cannot be conceived while disregarding the subject, even if its external existence is its existence in the subject. Rather, the realization of the essence of relation is that it is something adhering to the subject, i.e., the adhesion to the subject is constitutive of its essence because it is precisely the relation itself, unlike the other categories, for they are the state (al-hay'ah) that causes the relation to something. And if those categories were also from relation, then that relation would be in realization and the relation in the Metaphysics of the Healing (Ilahiyyat al-Shifa') in the chapter on the relative (al-mudaf) that the relative is not conceived by itself; it is always conceived with something to something. His saying: 'so that its determination' i.e., its realization as type, kind, or individual. His saying: 'for the realization of its attachment, not for the realization of its attached thing' because the effect of relation in existence [comes] through the realization of its attachment, and if the realization of the attachment is through the realization of the attached thing. His saying: 'according to the determination, etc.' — so type by type, kind by kind, individual by individual. His saying: 'the specific attachment as one thing' means the specific attachment is considered in terms of totality and unity in terms of detail and multiplicity, by considering it as a specific attachment, not in terms of being the attachment of this relation to this subject, and that is a discriminating factor that realizes the relation which is an ambiguous matter.
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of the nature of relation (al-idafah) as a category. It begins with the statement that relation does not subsist independently; its existence is not isolated but rather it is something that adheres to things. Consequently, its realization (tahassul) and specification (takhassus) are dependent on the realization of its attachment (luhuq) to something else and the specification of that attachment. The author then explains two ways to understand this. The first way is to take the attached thing (malhuq) and the relation together; in this case, the relation is determined according to the determination of the attached thing and the attachment. However, what is taken in this manner is not the category itself (al-maqulah), but a composite of the category and its substrate. The second way is to take the relation accompanied by the specific attachment as one restricted thing that attaches to the attached thing. This is the mode of diversification and realization of relation, and he notes that similarity (mushabahah) is union (ittihad). The commentary then clarifies the meaning of 'not having isolated existence': it does not mean that relation lacks external existence apart from its subject (since all accidents share that), but that its essence cannot be conceived in itself without reference to its subject; adhesion to the subject is constitutive of its essence because it is precisely relation itself, unlike other categories which are states that give rise to relation. The commentary references Ibn Sina's Metaphysics (Ilahiyyat al-Shifa') to support that the relative is always conceived with something to something. Further commentary explains that the determination of relation in terms of type, kind, or individual follows the determination of the attachment, and that the specific attachment can be considered in a general or detailed manner to actualize the otherwise ambiguous notion of relation.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'non-independence' here refers to lack of external existence, whereas it refers to the conceptual dependence of the essence on the subject.
Correction: The passage clarifies that all accidents are dependent on subjects in existence, but relation is unique in that its very essence cannot be conceived without reference to the subject; adhesion to the subject is constitutive of its definition.

Checkpoint

D) Relation is a mere mental construct.

Key Takeaway
Relation is uniquely defined by its attachment to a subject; its essence is not self-subsistent but realized through the bond.

When Reciprocity Requires a Relational Particle
A note on when reciprocity needs an explicit relational particle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the conditions under which a reciprocal relation requires an explicit relational particle in Arabic logical expressions.

Core Idea
Not all reciprocal relations need a particle – size comparisons are naturally reciprocal.

This short note clarifies an important qualification to the rule of reciprocity. While many reciprocal relations require an explicit relational particle, some, like comparative terms (e.g., 'bigness and smallness'), are self-reciprocal. The author gives examples to illustrate when the particle is the same on both sides and when it differs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to grasp the three cases presented.
Notice the compact style – it's a qualification inserted into a larger discussion.
2
Identify the three cases
Find the three categories: (1) no particle needed, (2) particle same on both sides, (3) particle different.
The examples are key: 'bigness and smallness', 'slave/master', 'knower/known'.
3
Reflect on the logic
Ask yourself why some relations (like comparison) are reciprocal without a particle, while others (like ownership or knowledge) need one.
This shows that linguistic expression mirrors the logical structure of relations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن الانعكاس قد لا يفتقر إلى اعتبار حرف النسبة كالعظم والصغر. وقد يفتقر إما على تساوي الحرف في الجانبين كقولنا : العبد عبد للمولى والمولى مولى للعبد، أو على اختلافه كقولنا : العالم عالم بالمعلوم والمعلوم معلوم للعالم .
And know that reflection (al-in'ikas) may not require the consideration of a relational particle, like 'bigness' and 'smallness'. And it may require it, either with equality of the particle on both sides, like our saying: 'the slave is a slave to the master and the master is a master to the slave', or with difference of it, like our saying: 'the knower is a knower of the known and the known is known to the knower'.
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage is a brief note that qualifies the earlier discussion on reciprocity (al-in'ikas). It states that sometimes the reciprocal relation can be expressed without an explicit relational particle (harf al-nisbah), as in the case of 'bigness' and 'smallness' (perhaps meaning comparative terms like 'bigger' and 'smaller' which imply relation without a separate particle). In other cases, a relational particle is required, and it may be the same on both sides (e.g., 'slave of master' and 'master of slave' both use the particle 'li-') or different (e.g., 'knower of the known' uses 'bi-' on one side and 'li-' on the other). The note highlights the conditions under which the reciprocity rule applies with regard to linguistic expression.

Key Terms

حرف النسبة
تساوي
اختلاف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that reciprocity always requires an explicit relational particle to express the relation in both directions.
Correction: Some relations, like comparative terms 'bigness and smallness', are reciprocal without requiring a particle because the terms themselves imply the relation.

Checkpoint

A) 'The slave is a slave to the master'
B) 'The knower is a knower of the known'
C) 'Bigness and smallness'
D) 'The father is father of the son'

Key Takeaway
Reciprocal relations may or may not require an explicit relational particle, depending on the nature of the relation.

Relation Has No Independent Existence
Defines relation as having no independent existence and explains its dependency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why relation (al-iḍāfa) is conceptually dependent and how it is conceived in two ways.

Core Idea
Relation cannot be conceived without reference to something else.

This passage tackles a subtle point in the study of categories: what does it mean for relation to have no independent existence? The author clarifies that this is not about external existence (all accidents share that) but about conceptual independence. By the end, you will see how relation's essence is to be a 'being-toward' another, and how it can be understood through two distinct approaches.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the immediate clarification that this is about conceptual, not external, existence.
2
Identify the main claim
The passage states that relation has no independent existence. Underline this and then note the clarification that follows.
3
Distinguish the two ways of conceiving relation
The passage presents two ways: (1) taking relatum and relation together, forming a composite, and (2) taking relation with specific inherence as one thing, giving diversity.
Think about how each way affects what we understand by 'relation'.
4
Connect to the misconception
The author warns against thinking this means relation has no external existence. Why is that warning important?
This guards against a common error.
