Relation does not have independent existence (i.e., it has no isolated existence so that its determination could be conceived by itself; rather its existence is that it is an attribute inhering in things). So its obtaining and specification are dependent on the obtaining of its inherence in the other and its specification. And that (i.e., its obtaining dependent on inherence) is understood sometimes by taking the subject of inherence and the relation together, so the relation is determined according to the determination of the subject of inherence and the inherence. And that taken in this way is not the category; rather it is something composite of the category and its substrate. And sometimes by taking the relation accompanied by the specific inherence as one thing restricted, attaching to that subject of inherence. And this is the diversity of relation and its obtaining, so similarity is the union. His saying: (i.e., it has no isolated existence, etc.) The intended meaning is not what is imagined from its apparent meaning that it has no existence in the external world separate from the subject, because all accidents are likewise, and because external existence has no role in the obtaining of the quiddity. Rather, the intended meaning is that it has no obtaining in itself and its quiddity is not conceived while disregarding the subject, even if its external existence is its existence in the subject. Rather, the obtaining of the quiddity of relation is that it is an attribute inhering in the subject, meaning inherence in the subject is constitutive of its quiddity because it is an expression of the relation itself, unlike the other categories, for they are expressions of the state caused by the relation in something. And if those categories were also expressions of the relation, then that relation would be in the obtaining and the relation in the Ilahiyyat of al-Shifa', in the chapter on the relative, that the relative is a thing not conceived by itself but is always conceived with something to something. His saying: (to conceive its determination i.e., its obtaining by type or species or individual. His saying: for the obtaining of its inherence not for the obtaining of its subject of inherence, due to a consequence of relation in existence, even though the obtaining of inherence is through the obtaining of the subject of inherence. His saying: according to the determination etc. i.e., by type according to type, by species according to species, by individual according to individual. His saying: the specific inherence as one thing means the specific inherence is considered in terms of entirety and unity in terms of detail and multiplicity, by considering that it is a specific inherence not in terms of it being the inherence of this relation to this subject, and that is a differentia that obtains the relation which is an ambiguous thing.
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of the nature of relation (al-iḍāfa) in the context of Aristotelian categories. It begins by stating that relation does not have independent existence, clarifying that its existence is always as an accident inhering in a subject. The author immediately addresses a potential misunderstanding: this lack of independent existence is not about external existence (since all accidents are dependent on a subject in external reality), but about conceptual independence. Relation's quiddity cannot be conceived without reference to something else; its essence is to be a relation (al-nisba) to another. The passage then explains two ways to conceive of relation: (1) taking the relatum and relation together, which yields a composite of the category and its substrate, and (2) taking the relation with its specific inherence as a single restricted thing, which gives the diversity of relation. The commentary elaborates on each phrase, emphasizing that the determination of relation follows the determination of its inherence, and that the specific inherence acts as a differentia that specifies the otherwise ambiguous notion of relation. The text also references Avicenna's Ilahiyyat to support the idea that the relative is always conceived with something else.

Inversion of Relation and Its Types
Classification of inversion into those requiring a particle and those not.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classification of inversion (conversion) of relational terms and the conditions under which a relational particle is needed.

Core Idea
Inversion of relation is automatic, but its expression may require a particle.

This short passage from a kalam text examines a logical-linguistic phenomenon: when we say something is related to something else, the relation holds both ways. But the way we express it can vary. Sanusi explains that some relations need a particle like 'of' or 'to' while others are expressed without one.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the three types
The passage presents three categories of inversion. Find each one and note the example given.
Pay attention to the role of the relational particle (ḥarf al-nisba).
3
Reflect on the classification
Consider why this classification might matter for logical reasoning. Think about how the same logical relation can be expressed differently in language.
This connects to the broader theme of language and logic in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن الانعكاس قد لا يفتقر إلى اعتبار حرف النسبة كالعظم والصغر. وقد يفتقر إما على تساوي الحرف في الجانبين كقولنا : العبد عبد للمولى والمولى مولى للعبد، أو على اختلافه كقولنا : العالم عالم بالمعلوم والمعلوم معلوم للعالم .
And know that inversion (al-inʿikās) may not require consideration of a particle of relation, like greatness and smallness. And it may require it, either with equality of the particle in both sides, as when we say: the slave is a slave to the master and the master is a master to the slave, or with its difference, as when we say: the knower is knower of the known and the known is known to the knower.
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage is a brief classification of the inversion (al-inʿikās) of relational terms. It states that sometimes the inversion of a relation does not need a relational particle (like 'of' or 'to') in the expression, as in the case of 'greater' and 'smaller' where the relation is implied by the terms themselves. In other cases, a relational particle is required. This can be subdivided: either the same particle is used on both sides (e.g., 'slave to master' and 'master to slave' both use the same preposition, here implied by the genitive construction in Arabic), or different particles are used (e.g., 'knower of' vs. 'known to'). The examples clarify that inversion is automatic: if A is related to B, then B is related to A, but the linguistic expression may vary.

Key Terms
انعكاس
حرف النسبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Inversion of relation always uses the same relational particle on both sides.
Correction: Inversion can be expressed without a particle, with the same particle, or with different particles depending on the relation.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of inversion that does not require a relational particle?
A) Slave and master
B) Greater and smaller
C) Knower and known
D) Father and son

Key Takeaway
Inversion of relation is logically automatic but linguistically may or may not require a particle, and the particle may be the same or different on both sides.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider your relationship with Allah. You are a slave to Allah, and He is your Master. Does the automatic inversion of this relation affect how you view your identity and purpose? How will you consciously live out both sides of this relation today?
?
Think of a personal relationship where you have failed to acknowledge the mutual aspect. If you are related to someone, they are equally related to you. Name one such relationship and commit to one action today that honors this mutual inversion.
?
The knower and known: you are known by Allah. Does that make you feel accountable? How does being fully known change your actions? What will you do today to live as someone who is consciously ‘known’ by your Creator?

Relation Determination Transfers Between Relata
Explains that if a relation is determinate in one relatum, it is also determinate in the other, but only when the relation itself is determined.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the principle that if a real relation is determinate in one relatum, it must also be determinate in the other, and the important distinction between determining the relation itself versus determining only its subject.

Core Idea
A relation's determination in one term forces determination in the other—but only for the relation itself, not its subject.

This passage from al-Mawaqif addresses a key nuance in the logic of relations. The author argues that if a relation is fully specified in one of its relata, it must be specified in the other as well. However, he carefully distinguishes between specifying the relation itself and specifying only its subject. This distinction has important implications for how we understand relational properties like 'half' and 'head-ness.'

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Notice the technical terms like 'تحصل' and 'إضافة حقيقية'.
The passage is concise; read carefully to follow the logical flow.
2
Identify the main principle
Find the sentence stating the principle: if the relation is determinate in one term, it is also determinate in the other. Also note the contrapositive: if it is absolute in one, it is absolute in the other.
3
Understand the logical justification
The author uses the law of contrapositive: if the consequent (determination in the other) is negated, the antecedent (determination in the first) is negated. This is why indeterminacy also transfers.
This is a standard logical move in kalam.
4
Examine the examples
Look at the half-double example: absolute half corresponds to absolute double, specific half to specific double. Then note the head-possessor example, which shows the exception when only the subject is determined.
The head example is crucial for the distinction.
5
Grasp the key distinction
The principle holds only when we determine the real relation itself (al-iḍāfa al-ḥaqīqiyya). Determining only the subject (e.g., a specific head as substance) does not determine the other relatum.
This refines our understanding of relational determination.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والموافقة ( في الكيف غير الكيف ) المتحد الموافق ) فإذا اعتبرنا الاتحاد والموافقة ( من حيث أنه في الكيف كان نوعاً من الإضافة المطلقة متحصلاً بحسب لحوقه للكيف، وكذا الحال في المساواة والمماثلة ) ثم الإضافة إذا كانت في طرف محصلة كانت في الطرف الآخر محصلة) أيضاً على حسب تحصيل الطرف الأول شخصياً كان أو نوعياً ( ويلزمه ) بسبب استلزام نقيض اللازم نقيض الملزوم ( أنها إذا كانت في طرف مطلقة) أي غير محصلة (ففي) الطرف الآخر مطلقة) أيضاً (فالنصف) المطلق ( في مقابلة الضعف ) المطلق ( وهذا النصف في مقابلة هذا الضعف ) فظهر أن أي المضافين عرف بالتحصيل والتعيين عرف الآخر به لكن (هذا إذا حصلنا نفس الإضافة ) الحقيقية كالنصفية والضعفية ( وأما إذا حصلنا موضوعها ) فقط (لم يلزم تحصيل المضاف المقابل له فتحصيل الرأس حتى يصير هذا الرأس لا يوجب تعيين من له رأس) يعني أن الرأسية إضافة عارضة بعضو مخصوص بالقياس إلى ذي الرأس، فإذا حصلنا ذلك العضو من حيث أنه جوهر معين حتى صار هذا الرأس لم يلزم تحصيل الشخص الذي هو ذو الرأس، نعم إذا حصلنا الرأسية التي هي الإضافة الحقيقية حتى تصير هذه الرأسية وجب أن تتحصل الإضافة في الطرف الآخر فيكون الرأس وذو الرأس متعينين حينئذ
And agreement (al-muwāfaqa) (in a quality different from the quality) the united agreeing. So if we consider union and agreement (in that it is in the quality, it is a type of absolute relation, obtained according to its inherence in the quality, and likewise the case in equality and similarity). Then, if the relation is determinate in one term, it is also determinate in the other term (also, according to the determination of the first term, whether individual or generic). And it follows (due to the necessity that the contradictory of the consequent entails the contradictory of the antecedent) that if it is absolute (i.e., indeterminate) in one term, then it is also absolute in the other term. So the absolute half is opposite the absolute double, and this half is opposite this double. Thus it appears that whichever of the two relata is known by determination and specification, the other is known by it. But (this is when we determine the relation itself (the real relation like half-ness and double-ness). As for when we determine only its subject, then the determination of the corresponding relatum does not follow. So the determination of the head until it becomes this head does not necessitate the specification of the one having a head. That is, head-ness is a relation attaching to a specific organ relative to the one having a head. So if we determine that organ in that it is a specific substance until it became this head, it does not necessitate the determination of the person who is the one having a head. Yes, if we determine the head-ness which is the real relation until this head-ness becomes specific, then the relation must be determinate in the other term, so the head and the one having a head become specified then.
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage is an argument about the determination of relation in both relata. It begins by discussing agreement (al-muwāfaqa) and union (al-ittiḥād) as types of relation in quality, stating that these are determinate relations. Then it establishes a key principle: if a relation is determinate (muḥaṣṣala) in one relatum, it is also determinate in the other, and conversely if it is indeterminate (muṭlaqa) in one, it is indeterminate in the other. This follows logically from the contradictory relationship. The author gives the example of half and double: absolute half is opposite absolute double, and a specific half is opposite a specific double. However, a crucial distinction is made: this only holds when we are dealing with the real relation itself (e.g., half-ness, double-ness). If we only determine the subject of the relation (e.g., a specific head as a substance), that does not necessitate determination of the other relatum (the one having a head). The final example clarifies: head-ness is a relation between a specific organ and the possessor of a head. If we determine that organ as a specific substance (this head), we still do not know the specific person having it; but if we determine the relation itself (this head-ness), then the other term (the possessor) is also determined.

Key Terms
تحصل
مقابلة
نقيض
موضوع
إضافة حقيقية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that determining the subject of a relation always determines the other relatum.
Correction: The passage clarifies that only determining the real relation itself forces determination in the other term; determining the subject alone does not.

Checkpoint
When does determining one relatum automatically determine the other relatum?
A) When the subject of the relation is determined
B) When the relation itself (e.g., half-ness) is determined
C) When both relata are substances
D) When the relation is indeterminate

Key Takeaway
A relation's full specification in one relatum guarantees specification in the other only when we are dealing with the relation itself, not its mere subject.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider a relationship in your life where you have focused on specifying the 'subject'—the external attributes or roles—rather than the 'relation itself' (the real connection). How has that affected your understanding of the other person? What would change if you shifted to specifying the relation itself, as the lesson teaches?
?
The lesson uses logical contrapositive to guard against error: if the relation is indeterminate in one term, it is indeterminate in the other. Can you think of a time when you assumed certainty about something (e.g., a theological truth) based on indeterminate premises? Where else might this logical oversight affect your convictions?
?
The example of head and possessor shows that knowing the head (subject) doesn’t give you the possessor. In your own journey of knowing Allah, are you sometimes content with knowing the 'subjects'—the creation—without seeking the real relation (tawḥīd) that connects you to the Creator? What is one step you can take today to specify that relation?

Divisions of Relation: Congruence and Opposition
First division: congruent vs divergent, with subcategories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first division of relation: whether the relation is congruent (same term on both sides) or divergent (different terms), and within divergent, the distinction between limited and unlimited.

Core Idea
Relations can be congruent or divergent; divergent relations are further divided into limited and unlimited.

Al-Sayyid al-Sharif al-Jurjani begins his discussion of the divisions of relation with a fundamental classification. Before we can analyze the nature of relations, we must distinguish whether the relational terms match or differ, and whether they apply to one specific counterpart or many. This passage lays out that foundational division.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the source Arabic and its literal translation carefully to get the author's own wording.
Notice the structure: first division presented as 'al-awwal'.
2
Identify the two main categories
Find where the passage says the relation is either 'congruent' (mutawāfiqa) or 'divergent' (mukhālifa). Understand the difference with examples.
Brotherhood is congruent, father-son is divergent.
3
Explore the sub-division of divergent
The passage further divides divergent into 'limited' (maḥdūd) and 'unlimited' (ghayr maḥdūd). Note how 'double' is limited while 'lesser' is unlimited.
Limited: one-to-one relation; unlimited: one-to-many.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this classification matters: it helps us understand the logical and ontological structure of relations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ المقصد الرابع : تقسيمات الإضافة ]
( يلحق الإضافة تقسيمات من وجوه الأول : إما أن تتوافق الإضافة (من الطرفين كالجوار والأخوة (وإما أن تتخالف كالابن (والأب) فإن البنوة والأبوة متخالفتان في الماهية ( والمتخالف إما محدود كالضعف والنصف ) فإن ضعفه شيء واحد تكون القياس إلى واحد آخر لا إلى أمور كثيرة وكذا النصفية ( أولا ) محدود كالأقل والأكثر فإن أقلية شيء واحد قد تكون بالقياس إلى أشياء متعددة وكذا الأكثرية ( الثاني : أنه قد تكون الإضافة (لصفة) موجودة (في كل واحدة من المضافين كالعشق فإنه لإدراك العاشق وجمال المعشوق ) فكل واحدة من العاشقية والمعشوقية إنما ثبت في محلها بواسطة صفة موجودة فيه ( أو لصفة في أحدهما ) فقط ( كالعالمية فإنها لصفة ) موجودة ( في العالم وهو العلم دون المعلوم) فإنه متصف بالمعلومية من غير أن يكون له صفة موجودة تقتضي اتصافه بها ( وإلا فللمعدوم بكونه معلوماً صفة موجودة ( وقد لا تكون الإضافة (لصفة) حقيقية ( أصلاً) أي في شيء من الطرفين ( كاليمين واليسار) إذ ليس للمتيامن صفة حقيقية
[The Fourth Purpose: Divisions of Relation]
Divisions attach to relation from aspects. The first: either the relation is congruent (from both sides, like adjacency and brotherhood) or it is divergent (like son and father, for sonship and fatherhood are divergent in quiddity). And the divergent is either limited (like double and half, for the doubling of one thing is a relation to one other, not to many things; likewise half-ness) or not limited (like the lesser and the greater, for the less-ness of one thing may be in relation to multiple things; likewise the greater-ness). The second: either the relation is due to an existing quality in each of the two relata (like love, for it is due to the perception of the lover and the beauty of the beloved); so each of lover-ness and beloved-ness is established in its locus by means of an existing quality in it. Or it is due to a quality in only one of them (like knowledge, for it is due to an existing quality in the knower, namely knowledge, not in the known); for the known is characterized by being-known without having an existing quality that necessitates its being so characterized—otherwise the non-existent, by being known, would have an existing quality. Or the relation may not be due to any real quality at all (i.e., in either of the two sides), like right and left, for the right-sided has no real quality.
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of divisions (taqsīmāt) of relation. It presents two main classifications. The first division is based on whether the relation from both sides is congruent (mutawāfiqa) or divergent (mukhālifa). Congruent relations are those where the relation term is the same on both sides (e.g., neighbor is neighbor to neighbor, brother is brother to brother). Divergent relations are those where the terms differ in quiddity (e.g., son and father). Within divergent relations, there is a further subdivision: limited (maḥdūd) where the relation relates one thing to a single other (e.g., double and half each refer to a specific counterpart), and unlimited (ghayr maḥdūd) where the relation can relate to multiple things (e.g., lesser and greater can apply to many comparisons). The second division is based on the basis of the relation: it can be due to an existing quality in both relata (e.g., love, which requires the lover's perception and the beloved's beauty), or due to a quality in only one relatum (e.g., knowledge, which exists in the knower but not in the known—the known simply has the attribute of being-known without a real quality), or not due to any real quality at all (e.g., right and left, which are purely relational without a real quality in either side). The commentary notes that if being-known required a quality in the known, then non-existent objects would have a quality, which is absurd.

Key Terms
تقسيم
توافق
تخالف
محدود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that all relations are symmetrical (like brotherhood).
Correction: The passage shows that divergent relations have different terms on each side, e.g., father and son.

Checkpoint
According to the first division, which type of relation is 'lesser' compared to 'greater'?
A) Congruent and limited
B) Divergent and limited
C) Divergent and unlimited
D) Congruent and unlimited

Key Takeaway
The first division of relation distinguishes congruent (same-term) and divergent (different-term) relations, with divergent further divided into limited (one-to-one) and unlimited (one-to-many).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a relationship where you are a son or daughter. The lesson describes this as a 'divergent' relation—your role is different from your parent's. Do you truly live out this asymmetry in responsibility and honor, or do you unconsciously expect congruence? Today, identify one specific way you can act upon this asymmetry to fulfill your duty.
?
Consider an opinion you hold strongly. Do you treat it as a 'limited' relation—true only in one specific comparison—or do you act as if it is 'unlimited,' applicable to everyone? The lesson shows that some relations are limited to one counterpart. Are you assuming your perspective is universally valid when it might only be relative to one factor? Will you examine one belief today to see if it is truly limited?

Divisions of Relation: Basis in Real Qualities
Second division: three cases based on real qualities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second division of relation based on whether the relation is due to a real quality in both relata, in one relatum, or in neither.

Core Idea
Relations may be grounded in a real quality in both, one, or neither of the relata.

After classifying relations by agreement/opposition, al-Jurjani turns to a different aspect: the basis of the relation. He examines whether a real quality exists in both sides, only one, or neither. This reveals fundamental differences in how relations are grounded in reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the second division
Read the Arabic and translation for the second division, noting the three cases presented.
2
Identify the three cases
Find where the passage states: relation due to a quality in both relata, in only one, or in neither.
Examples: love (both), knowledge (one), right/left (none).
3
Understand the argument about non-existent beings
The passage notes that if being-known required a quality in the known, then non-existent objects would have a quality, which is absurd.
This supports the second case: knowledge is due to a quality only in the knower.
4
Reflect on the metaphysical implications
Consider how this classification challenges the assumption that all relations involve real qualities in both terms.
Some relations are purely relational without inherent qualities.

Specification of Relation in One Term Requires Specification in the Other
Argument that specification of the relation itself necessitates specification of both terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the principle that specifying a relation in one term necessitates specifying it in the other, and the crucial distinction between specifying the relation itself and specifying only its subject.

Core Idea
Relation specification is a two-way street, but only when you specify the relation itself, not just its subject.

This passage from Sanusi's commentary tackles a subtle point about relations. When we talk about relations like half-ness or head-ness, does specifying one term automatically pin down the other? The answer is yes and no, depending on what exactly we are specifying. This lesson will walk through the rule and its important qualification.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the general claim at the start.
Pay attention to the terms 'muhassala' (specified) and 'mutlaqa' (absolute).
2
Identify the rule and illustration
The author states that specification of one term requires specification of the other, and illustrates with half and double.
3
Understand the qualification
The author then qualifies: this holds only when specifying the relation itself, not its subject, as shown with the head example.
Distinguish between specifying 'head-ness' (the relation) and 'the head' (the subject).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الإضافة إذا كانت في طرف محصلة كانت في الطرف الآخر محصلة أيضاً على حسب تحصيل الطرف الأول شخصياً كان أو نوعياً ويلزمه أنها إذا كانت في طرف مطلقة أي غير محصلة ففي الطرف الآخر مطلقة أيضاً فالنصف المطلق في مقابلة الضعف المطلق وهذا النصف في مقابلة هذا الضعف فظهر أن أي المضافين عرف بالتحصيل والتعيين عرف الآخر به لكن هذا إذا حصلنا نفس الإضافة الحقيقية كالنصفية والضعفية وأما إذا حصلنا موضوعها فقط لم يلزم تحصيل المضاف المقابل له فتحصيل الرأس حتى يصير هذا الرأس لا يوجب تعيين من له رأس يعني أن الرأسية إضافة عارضة بعضو مخصوص بالقياس إلى ذي الرأس، فإذا حصلنا ذلك العضو من حيث أنه جوهر معين حتى صار هذا الرأس لم يلزم تحصيل الشخص الذي هو ذو الرأس، نعم إذا حصلنا الرأسية التي هي الإضافة الحقيقية حتى تصير هذه الرأسية وجب أن تتحصل الإضافة في الطرف الآخر فيكون الرأس وذو الرأس متعينين حينئذ
Then if the relation (al-idafa) is specified (muhassala) in one term, it is also specified in the other term, in accordance with the specification of the first term, whether it is individual or generic. And it follows from this that if it is absolute (mutlaqa) in a term, i.e., unspecified, then in the other term it is also absolute. So the absolute half is in contrast to the absolute double, and this specific half is in contrast to this specific double. Thus it appears that whichever of the two relatives is known by specification and determination, the other is known by it. But this is when we specify the relation (al-idafa) itself, like half-ness and double-ness. However, if we specify only its subject, the specification of the other relative does not follow. So specifying the head so that it becomes this head does not necessitate determining the one who has a head, meaning that head-ness (al-ra'asiyya) is a relation accidental to a particular member relative to the possessor of the head. If we specify that member as a determined substance so that it becomes this head, the person who is the possessor of the head does not become specified. Yes, if we specify head-ness, which is the real relation, such that this head-ness is specified, then the relation must be specified in the other term, and then the head and the possessor of the head become determined.
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage is an argument about how specification works in relations. The author first states the general rule: if a relation is specified in one term, it is also specified in the other, whether the specification is individual or generic. He illustrates with the example of 'half' and 'double': an absolute half corresponds to an absolute double, and a specific half corresponds to a specific double. So knowing one relative determines the other. However, he then qualifies this: this applies only when we specify the relation itself (e.g., the actual quality of half-ness or double-ness). If we specify merely the subject of the relation (e.g., the head as a substance), that does not require the counterpart (the possessor of the head) to be specified. He explains that head-ness is a relation that inheres in the head as a determinate substance, but the head's specification as 'this head' does not force the person to be known. Only when the relation itself (head-ness) is specified does it demand specification of both terms.

Key Terms
الإضافة
محصلة
مطلقة
الرأسية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that specifying any relative term always leads to specification of the other term.
Correction: The passage clarifies that this is only true when we specify the relation itself, not merely its subject.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when does specification of one relative necessitate specification of the other?
A) When the subject of the relation is specified
B) When the relation itself is specified
C) When the terms are absolute
D) In all cases without exception

Key Takeaway
Specifying the relation itself forces specification of both terms, but specifying only the subject does not.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often think that by perfecting one side of a relationship, the other side automatically adjusts. But the passage distinguishes between specifying the relation itself and specifying only the subject. Reflect on a relationship in your life where you focused on your own qualities without defining the nature of the bond. How can you now define the relation itself (e.g., love, obedience, partnership) to bring clarity and responsibility?
?
The passage uses the example of a head and its possessor. You might think that your physical body or your status (subject) defines who you are in relation to God. But Allah considers the real relation (submission, faith) that specifies both terms. Have you been relying on external identifications (like being a Muslim by name) without specifying the real relation of servitude? What step can you take today to specify that real relation?

Classification of Relations: Concordant, Discordant, Limited, Unlimited
Classification of relations into concordant/discordant and limited/unlimited.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how relations are classified into concordant vs discordant and, within discordant, into limited vs unlimited, with examples.

Core Idea
Relations are either concordant or discordant; discordant ones are either limited or unlimited.

This passage introduces the first major division of relations (idafa) in logic and ontology. Understanding this classification is essential for analyzing how things relate to each other in terms of mutual or one-sided connections, and whether the relation is defined by a single counterpart or many. Let's examine the text.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get an overall sense. Then read the English translation carefully. Pay attention to the examples.
The passage is short but dense with terminology.
2
Identify the two main categories
Note the first division: concordant (mutawafiqa) vs discordant (mukhtalifa). Look at the examples: neighborliness/brotherhood (concordant) and father/son (discordant). Ask yourself why these examples fit.
Concordant means the relation is the same from both sides; discordant means the two sides are different in essence.
3
Examine the sub-division of discordant
Within discordant, the author divides into limited (mahdud) and unlimited (ghayr mahdud). Notice the criterion: a limited relation has a single counterpart (e.g., double relates to one half), while an unlimited relation can have multiple counterparts (e.g., less than many things).
This distinction is crucial for logical analysis.
4
Reflect on the importance
Consider why this classification matters: it helps us understand how properties like double, half, more, less are relational and how they depend on the number of relata.
This classification is foundational for discussions in metaphysics and logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يلحق الإضافة تقسيمات من وجوه الأول : إما أن تتوافق الإضافة من الطرفين كالجوار والأخوة وإما أن تتخالف كالابن والأب فإن البنوة والأبوة متخالفتان في الماهية والمتخالف إما محدود كالضعف والنصف فإن ضعفه شيء واحد تكون القياس إلى واحد آخر لا إلى أمور كثيرة وكذا النصفية وإما لا محدود كالأقل والأكثر فإن أقلية شيء واحد قد تكون بالقياس إلى أشياء متعددة وكذا الأكثرية
Divisions (taqsimat) attach to relation (al-idafa) from several aspects: The first: either the relation is concordant (mutawafiqa) from both sides, like neighborliness and brotherhood; or it is discordant (mukhtalifa), like son and father, for sonship and fatherhood are discordant in essence. And the discordant is either limited (mahdud), like double and half, for the double of something is one thing measured relative to one other thing, not to many things; similarly half-ness; or unlimited (ghayr mahdud), like the less and the more, for the less-ness of one thing may be relative to many things, and similarly more-ness.
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage classifies relations (idafa) from the first aspect. The author divides relations into two types: concordant (where the relation from both sides is the same in meaning, like neighborliness and brotherhood) and discordant (where the relations are different, like sonship and fatherhood). Then within discordant, he further divides into limited and unlimited. Limited means the relation is defined by a single counterpart, such as double or half, where a specific double relates to one specific half (or vice versa). Unlimited means the relation can hold relative to multiple counterparts, like one thing being less than many others, or more than many others.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Limited means the relation is small in scope or magnitude.
Correction: Limited means the relation's counterpart is a single determinate thing, not multiple things.

Checkpoint
The relation of 'double' (al-di'f) is classified as what kind of discordant relation?
A) Concordant
B) Discordant and limited
C) Discordant and unlimited
D) Neither concordant nor discordant

Key Takeaway
This classification shows that relations can be symmetric (concordant) or asymmetric (discordant), and asymmetric relations can have a single or multiple counterparts.

The Second Division: Relations Based on Attribute Presence in Both or One Party
Differentiation of relations based on whether the attribute causing the relation exists in both or only one party.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how relations are classified based on whether the attribute that grounds the relation exists in both relatives or only in one.

Core Idea
Relations can arise from an attribute in both parties, or in only one.

After discussing relations based on existence (positive/negative/equivocal), the author now introduces a second division: does the attribute causing the relation exist in both relatives or only one? This distinction is crucial for understanding how qualities like love and knowledge relate their subjects to objects. In this lesson, we will explore two clear examples that illustrate the difference.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: 'It may be... or...'
The passage is concise; each word matters.
2
Understand the first case: attribute in both parties
The example of love ('ishq) shows that both the lover and the beloved have an attribute: the lover has perception, the beloved has beauty. Thus both 'lover-ness' and 'beloved-ness' are grounded in real attributes.
Note that the relation is symmetrical in terms of attribute presence.
3
Understand the second case: attribute in only one party
The example of knowledge ('alimiyya) shows that only the knower has an attribute (knowledge). The known object is described as 'known' without having an existing attribute; otherwise, a non-existent thing that is known would have to have an attribute, which is absurd.
This refutes the idea that being known requires a real attribute in the known object.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : أنه قد تكون الإضافة لصفة موجودة في كل واحدة من المضافين كالعشق فإنه لإدراك العاشق وجمال المعشوق فكل واحدة من العاشقية والمعشوقية إنما ثبت في محلها بواسطة صفة موجودة فيه أو لصفة في أحدهما فقط كالعالمية فإنها لصفة موجودة في العالم وهو العلم دون المعلوم فإنه متصف بالمعلومية من غير أن يكون له صفة موجودة تقتضي اتصافه بها وإلا فللمعدوم بكونه معلوماً صفة موجودة
The second: it may be that the relation is due to an attribute (sifa) existing in each of the two relatives, like love ('ishq), for it is due to the perception of the lover and the beauty of the beloved; so each of lover-ness and beloved-ness is established in its locus by means of an attribute existing in it. Or due to an attribute in one of them only, like worldliness ('alimiyya), for it is due to an attribute existing in the knower, which is knowledge, not in the known object, for it is described as being known without having an existing attribute that necessitates its being so described; otherwise the non-existent would, by being known, have an existing attribute.
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage presents the second division of relations, based on the presence of the attribute that grounds the relation. The first case is when the relation arises from an attribute present in both relatives, exemplified by love ('ishq): the lover has perception, the beloved has beauty, so the relations 'lover-ness' and 'beloved-ness' each depend on an attribute in their subject. The second case is when the relation originates from an attribute in only one of the two relatives, such as the relation 'knower' (al-'alimiyya): it is based on knowledge existing in the knower, while the known object does not have a separate existing attribute that makes it known; it is merely the object of knowledge. The author adds an important qualification: if the known object required an existent attribute, then a non-existent thing that is known would have to have an existing attribute, which is absurd. So the known object's being known is a pure relation without a real attribute.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the known object must have an attribute of 'being known' in the same way the knower has knowledge.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the known object does not need an existing attribute to be described as 'known'; otherwise non-existents would have attributes, which is absurd.

Checkpoint
Why does the author mention the non-existent (al-ma'dum) in this passage?
A) To prove that non-existents have attributes
B) To show that love can exist without attributes
C) To refute the idea that the known object must have an existing attribute
D) To argue that relations require attributes in both terms

Key Takeaway
Relations can be grounded in an attribute present in both relatives (like love) or in only one (like knowledge), and the known object does not require a real attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about being known by Allah, do you feel pressured to acquire certain attributes to be worthy of His knowledge? This lesson shows that being known does not require you to have an attribute—you can simply be known. How does that free you from striving for a self-image and allow you to rest in His omniscience?
?
Consider a relationship where you have given much but received little. The example of love requires attributes from both, but knowledge is unilateral. Are there relationships in your life where you expect the other to contribute an attribute they don't have? How can you adjust your expectations based on the type of relation this lesson describes?

Ibn Sina's Classification of Relations
Ibn Sina's enumeration of relation types: equality, action/passion, imitation, and union, with commentary on each.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's classification of relations into four categories: equality, action/passion, imitation, and union, and how the expanded version from al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya refines these with the addition of 'increase'.

Core Idea
Ibn Sina reduces all relations to four types: equality, action/passion, imitation, and union.

Ibn Sina's classification of relations is a foundational topic in Islamic philosophy and theology, providing a framework for understanding how things are connected. This passage presents his classification in both a concise and an expanded form, along with commentary that clarifies each division. Pay careful attention to the examples given, as they illustrate the logical basis for each category.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the difference between the concise and expanded versions.
2
Identify the two classifications
The passage first gives a concise fourfold division: equality, action/passion, imitation, union. Then it gives an expanded version from al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya: equality, increase, action/passion with source from power, and imitation. Compare them.
Union is absent in the expanded version; some of its items are under equality.
3
Examine the commentary examples
For each category, note the examples: e.g., for equality: dominant, vanquisher; for action/passion: cutting, breaking; for imitation: knowledge and report; for increase: quantity-based like little/much, power-based like dominant/manifest.
The examples ground the abstract categories in concrete relations.
4
Reflect on the principle of 'increase' and 'source'
The expanded version includes 'increase' as a category, which is subdivided into quantity and power. The commentary explains 'source' (masdar) as either the origination or the principle of action/passion. Understand why this matters for the completeness of the classification.
The category of increase captures relational asymmetry based on quantitative or qualitative superiority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : قال ابن سينا تكاد الإضافة تنحصر في أقسام في المعادلة كالغالب والقاهر والمانع، وفي الفعل والانفعال كالقطع والكسر، وفي المحاكاة كالعلم والخبر وفي الاتحاد كالمجاورة والمشابهة والمماثلة والمساواة واعلم أن المنقول في المباحث المشرقية من كلامه هو :هكذا تكاد تكون المضافات منحصرة في أقسام المعادلة والتي بالزيادة والتي بالفعل والانفعال ومصدرهما من القوة والتي بالمحاكاة، فأما التي بالزيادة فإما من الكم وهو ظاهر وإما من القوة كالغالب والقاهر والمانع، وأما التي بالفعل والانفعال فكالأب والابن والقاطع والمنقطع، وأما التي بالمحاكاة فكالعلم والمعلوم والحس والمحسوس، فإن العلم يحاكي هيئة المعلوم والحس يحاكي هيئة المحسوس، على أن ذلك لا يضبط تقديره ولا يلتبس عليك أنه لو قوله : ( في المعادلة) وهي أن يكون كل منهما عديلاً ونظيراً للآخر في التاج المعادلة بآخر برابر بودن ويدخل فيها كل إضافة تكون لأمرين نظراً للآخر وعديلاً له كالمساواة والمشابهة والمماثلة والمخالفة والمضادة والمقابلة حتى مطلق الزيادة والنقصان . قوله : ( ومصدرها) بالجر وبإفراد الضمير وهو الموافق للشفاء والمباحث المشرقية فيرجع إلى لفظ التي وفي بعضها بتثنية الضمير، فيرجع إلى الفعل والانفعال والمصدر إما بمعنى الصدور والمعنى والتي سبب صدورها من القوة إلى مبدأ التغير فيكون عطفه قريباً من العطف التفسيري ويؤيده عدم إيراد مثال له وإما بمعنى مبدأ الصدور ومن القوة بيان له والمعنى والتي بسبب مبدأ الفعل والانفعال كالأشد تأثيراً وتأثراً، فإنه سبب القوة التي هي سبب التأثير والتأثر. قوله : ( والتي بالمحاكات في التاج المحاكات خبري را حکایت کردن وأصل المحاكات المشابهة التي تكون بسبب كون شيء حكاية عن شيء. قوله : ( فإما من الكم بكسر إما . قوله : ( وهو ظاهر كالقليل والكثير والضعف والنصف والطويل والقصير والعظيم والصغير وغير ذلك وفي الشفاء بدل وهو ظاهر مستقيم. قوله : ( وإما من القوة بل إما من الكم فالفاء في قوله فكالغالب عاطفة أو زائدة لأجزائه، وفي بعض النسخ فهو ظاهر فحينئذ كلمة إما في الموضعين شرطية والفاء جزائية . قوله : ( كالغالب إلخ) فإن الغالبية والمغلوبية والقاهرية والمانعية إضافة بسبب زيادة القوة أي مبدأ التأثير والتأثر ونقصانها . قوله : ( فكالأب والابن فإنهما حاصلتان بسبب إلقاء النطفة في الرحم وقبوله إياها . قوله : ( والقاطع والمنقطع فإن القطع والانقطاع سببان لحصول القاطعية والمنقطعية اللتين من الإضافات . قوله : ( فكالعلم والمعلوم أي العالمية والمعلومية فإن هاتين حاصلتان بسبب كون العلم حكاية المعلوم .
The third: Ibn Sina said: 'Relation nearly restricts itself to divisions: in equality (al-mu'adala) like the dominant, the vanquisher, and the preventer; in action and passion (al-fi'l wa al-infi'al) like cutting and breaking; in imitation (al-muhakat) like knowledge and report; and in union (al-ittihad) like adjacency, similarity, likeness, and equality.' And know that what is transmitted in al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya from his speech is thus: 'The relatives are nearly restricted to divisions of equality, and those of increase, and those of action and passion and their source from power, and those of imitation. As for those of increase, they are either from quantity (al-kamm) and that is apparent, or from power like the dominant, the vanquisher, and the preventer. As for those of action and passion, they are like the father and the son, and the cutter and the cut. As for those of imitation, they are like knowledge and the known, and sense and the sensed, for knowledge imitates the form of the known and sense imitates the form of the sensed.' With the remark that that cannot be precisely determined, and do not confuse yourself that if... His statement: (in equality) meaning that each is the equal and peer of the other; in the Taj, al-mu'adala means being equal to another; it includes every relation that is between two things in consideration of the other and being its peer, like equality, similarity, likeness, difference, opposition, contrast, even absolute increase and decrease. His statement: (and their source) with a kasra and singular pronoun, which agrees with al-Shifa' and al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya, so it refers to the word 'those' (allati), and in some copies with dual pronoun, so it refers to action and passion; and 'source' (al-masdar) either means origination, so the meaning is 'those whose cause of origination from power to the principle of change', so it is near to an explicative conjunction, supported by not giving an example; or it means the principle of origination, and 'from power' is an explanation of it, and the meaning is 'those due to the principle of action and passion', like the more effective and more affected, for it is the cause of the power which is the cause of affecting and being affected. His statement: (and those of imitation) in the Taj: al-muhakat means reporting a report; the origin of al-muhakat is similarity which occurs due to something being a report of something. His statement: (so either from quantity) with a kasra on 'imma'. His statement: (and it is apparent like the little and the much, the double and the half, the long and the short, the great and the small, and others; and in al-Shifa' instead of 'and it is apparent' is 'straightforward'. His statement: (or from power; nay rather from quantity? so the fa in his saying 'so like the dominant' is conjunctive or extra for its parts, and in some copies it is 'so it is apparent', then the word 'imma' in both places is conditional and the fa is the response. His statement: (like the dominant etc.) for dominance, being dominated, vanquisher-ness, and preventer-ness are relations due to increase and decrease of power, i.e., the principle of affecting and being affected. His statement: (like the father and the son) for they arise due to the depositing of the sperm in the womb and its acceptance. His statement: (and the cutter and the cut) for cutting and being cut are the causes for the occurrence of cutter-ness and cut-ness, which are relations. His statement: (like knowledge and the known) i.e., knower-ness and known-ness, for these two arise due to knowledge being an imitation of the known.
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Sina's classification of relations, first in a concise form and then in an expanded form from his al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya. The initial list includes four divisions: equality (mu'adala, relations of mutual opposition like dominant, vanquisher, preventer), action and passion (like cutting and breaking), imitation (like knowledge imitating the known), and union (like adjacency, similarity, equality). The expanded version from al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya reorganizes into: equality, those of increase (which is either quantitative or based on power), those of action and passion (including their source from power), and those of imitation. The commentary then glosses each term. 'Equality' (mu'adala) means each thing is the counterpart and peer of the other; it includes all symmetrical relations like similarity, difference, opposition, etc., even absolute increase or decrease (which are relations of being more or less). 'Source' (masdar) in 'their source from power' is explained: it could mean 'origination' (the cause of the action and passion arising from power) or 'principle' (the power itself as the source of action). Examples: the dominant, vanquisher, preventer are relations due to superior power. For action and passion, father and son arise from the act of depositing sperm and acceptance; cutter and cut arise from cutting and being cut. For imitation, knowledge and the known are related because knowledge represents the form of the known; sense and the sensed are similar.

الفعل والانفعال
المحاكاة
الاتحاد
الزيادة
القوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ibn Sina's classification of relations is exhaustive and strictly precise.
Correction: The commentary explicitly states that the classification cannot be precisely determined ('لا يضبط تقديره'), indicating it is an approximation.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Sina as presented, the relation between father and son belongs to which category?
A) Equality (al-mu'adala)
B) Those of increase (allati bi al-ziyada)
C) Action and passion (al-fi'l wa al-infi'al)
D) Imitation (al-muhakat)

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina's classification of relations is an important philosophical tool, but it is not exhaustive; it provides a framework for understanding how things relate through equality, action/passion, imitation, and increase.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

What one relationship will you consciously surrender to Allah this week?
?
The commentary admits Ibn Sina's classification is not exhaustive and cannot be precisely determined. When you label or categorize people (e.g., 'enemy', 'friend', 'superior'), do you leave room for that uncertainty? How often do you reduce someone to a relation rather than seeing them as a whole person created by Allah? Will you commit to seeing one person this week beyond the labels you've placed on them?
?
Knowledge is described as an imitation of the known. Is your knowledge of your own shortcomings merely a report—or does it truly reflect your state? If it is just imitation, what would it take for you to truly 'represent' obedience to Allah in your actions today?

Ibn Sina's Classification of Relations into Four Types
Ibn Sina classifies relations into four categories with examples and notes the approximate nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's four-fold classification of relations (al-idafah) and the approximate nature of this enumeration.

Core Idea
Relations are almost confined to four categories: equivalence, increase/subjection, action/passion, and representation.

Ibn Sina offers a concise taxonomy of relations in metaphysics. Recognizing that relations are diverse, he proposes that they can be grouped into a handful of fundamental types. In this passage, you will encounter the four-part classification as well as a slightly different version from his Eastern Investigations, together with illustrative examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the initial classification
Start with Ibn Sina's statement listing four types: mu'adalat, fi'l/infi'al, muhakat, ittihad. Note the examples.
The examples help clarify each type.
2
Compare with the Eastern Investigations version
Notice that in the Eastern Investigations, the fourth type is replaced by 'ziyadah' (increase) and 'ittihad' is omitted, and instead there is 'source from power'.
This shows the fluidity of the classification.
3
Examine the subcategories
The 'increase' type is divided into quantity and power. The 'action/passion' type is exemplified by father/son and cutter/cut. The 'representation' type includes knowledge/known and sense/sensed.
These examples are concrete and from everyday experience.
4
Understand why 'almost confined'
Ibn Sina uses 'almost' to indicate the classification is not exhaustive. The commentator notes it cannot be precisely delimited.
This humility is important in philosophical taxonomies.
5
Reflect on the significance
This classification helps in analyzing how things relate to each other in the world, and it reappears in later Islamic philosophy.
It is a foundational taxonomy.

الثالث : قال ابن سينا تكاد الإضافة تنحصر في أقسام في المعادلة كالغالب والقاهر والمانع، وفي الفعل والانفعال كالقطع والكسر، وفي المحاكاة كالعلم والخبر وفي الاتحاد كالمجاورة والمشابهة ) والمماثلة والمساواة واعلم أن المنقول في المباحث المشرقية من كلامه هو :هكذا تكاد تكون المضافات منحصرة في أقسام المعادلة والتي بالزيادة والتي بالفعل والانفعال ومصدرهما من القوة والتي بالمحاكاة، فأما التي بالزيادة فإما من الكم وهو ظاهر وإما من القوة كالغالب والقاهر والمانع، وأما التي بالفعل والانفعال فكالأب والابن والقاطع والمنقطع، وأما التي بالمحاكاة فكالعلم والمعلوم والحس والمحسوس، فإن العلم يحاكي هيئة المعلوم والحس يحاكي هيئة المحسوس، على أن ذلك لا يضبط تقديره ولا يلتبس عليك أنه لو
قوله : ( في المعادلة) وهي أن يكون كل منهما عديلاً ونظيراً للآخر في التاج المعادلة بآخر برابر بودن ويدخل فيها كل إضافة تكون لأمرين نظراً للآخر وعديلاً له كالمساواة والمشابهة والمماثلة والمخالفة والمضادة والمقابلة حتى مطلق الزيادة والنقصان .
قوله : ( ومصدرها) بالجر وبإفراد الضمير وهو الموافق للشفاء والمباحث المشرقية فيرجع إلى لفظ التي وفي بعضها بتثنية الضمير، فيرجع إلى الفعل والانفعال والمصدر إما بمعنى الصدور والمعنى والتي سبب صدورها من القوة إلى مبدأ التغير فيكون عطفه قريباً من العطف التفسيري ويؤيده عدم إيراد مثال ،له وإما بمعنى مبدأ الصدور ومن القوة بيان له والمعنى والتي بسبب مبدأ الفعل والانفعال كالأشد تأثيراً وتأثراً، فإنه سبب القوة التي هي سبب التأثير والتأثر. قوله : ( والتي بالمحاكات في التاج المحاكات خبري را حکایت کردن وأصل المحاكات

اللتين من الإضافات .
قوله : ( فكالعلم والمعلوم أي العالمية والمعلومية فإن هاتين حاصلتان بسبب كون العلم حكاية المعلوم .
على أن ذلك لا يضبط تقديره إشارة إلى كونها منحصرة في أقسام في الشفاء على أن هذا لا يضبط تقديره وتحديده إشارة إلى المحاكات التي هي المذكورة عن قريب ففيه منه على كمال انتشار وعلى التقديرين متعلق بقوله تكاد أي إنما قلنا تكاد ينحصر وما خير هنا بالحصر بناء على أنه لا يمكن إيراده بوجه الضبط .
قوله : ( لتطابق) المنقولات أي منقول المتن ومنقول المباحث المشرقية أو المنقول والمنقول عنه أعني كلامه في الشفاء.
ثم بدل في عبارة الكتاب لفظ المعادلة بلفظ الزيادة لتطابق المنقولان بحسب المعنى، إذ يكون حينئذ قوله : وفي الاتحاد قائماً مقام المعادلة وإما وقوع الخبر موقع الحس فلا بأس به، لأن الخبر أيضاً حكاية هيئة المخبر عنه
Third: Ibn Sina said: relation is almost confined to divisions in mu'adalat (equivalence), like the dominant, the overpowering, and the preventer; and in action and passion, like cutting and breaking; and in muhakat (representation), like knowledge and report; and in ittihad (union), like adjacency, similarity, likeness, and equality. Know that what is transmitted in the Eastern Investigations from his speech is thus: the relations are almost confined to divisions of mu'adalat, and those by increase, and those by action and passion and their source from power, and those by representation. As for those by increase, they are either from quantity—and it is apparent—or from power, like the dominant, the overpowering, and the preventer. As for those by action and passion, like the father and the son, and the cutter and the cut. As for those by representation, like knowledge and the known, and sense and the sensed, for knowledge represents the form of the known, and sense represents the form of the sensed—though that is not precisely delimitable, and it is not obscure to you that if... His saying: (in mu'adalat): that each of them is equal and counterpart of the other. In the Taj, mu'adalat is being equal with another. It includes every relation that occurs between two things in view of the other and as a counterpart, such as equality, similarity, likeness, difference, opposition, and contrast, even absolute increase and decrease. His saying: (and their source) is in the genitive and with the singular pronoun, which conforms to the Shifa and the Eastern Investigations, so it refers to the word 'those'; and in some copies with the dual pronoun, thus referring to action and passion. The source (masdar) is either in the sense of emanation (sudūr), meaning 'those whose emanation is from power to the principle of change', so the conjunction is close to epexegetical, supported by the lack of an example for it; or in the sense of the beginning of emanation (mabda' al-sudūr), and 'from power' is an explanation of it, meaning 'those which are due to the beginning of action and passion', like the more intense in effect and affect, for it is the cause of the power which is the cause of effect and affect. His saying: (and those by muhakat). In the Taj: muhakat is to narrate a report, and the origin of muhakat is similarity that occurs due to something being a representation of something. His saying: (so either from quantity) with kasra on 'imma'. His saying: (and it is apparent) like the little and the many, the double and the half, the long and the short, the great and the small, and so on. In the Shifa, instead of 'and it is apparent' it is 'straight'. His saying: (or from power—rather from quantity). The fa in 'fa-ka'l-ghalib' is either explanatory or redundant for its parts. In some copies it is 'and it is apparent', so then 'imma' in both positions is conditional and the fa is resulative. His saying: (like the dominant, etc.): for dominativeness and subjugatedness, overpoweringness and preventiveness are relations due to increase of power—that is, the beginning of effect and affect—and its decrease. His saying: (like the father and the son): for they obtain due to the deposition of the sperm in the womb and its reception. His saying: (and the cutter and the cut): for cutting and being cut are two causes for the occurrence of cutter-ness and cut-ness, which are among relations. His saying: (like knowledge and the known): that is, knower-ness and known-ness, for these two obtain due to knowledge being a representation of the known. 'Though that is not precisely delimitable' refers to its being confined to divisions. In the Shifa: 'though this is not precisely delimitable and determinable' refers to the muhakat mentioned recently, so it contains a criticism of its complete diffusion. In both estimates it is attached to His saying 'almost'—that is, we only said 'almost' confined, and we did not choose to assert confinement because it cannot be presented in a precise manner. His saying: (to match) the transmissions, i.e., the transmission of the text and the transmission of the Eastern Investigations, or the transmission and the transmitted-from, i.e., his speech in the Shifa. Then he replaced in the wording of the book the word mu'adalat with the word ziyadah (increase) so that the two transmissions correspond in meaning, for then his saying 'and in union' takes the place of mu'adalat. And as for occurrence of report in the place of sense, there is no harm in it, because the report is also a representation of the state of the reported.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Sina's classification of relations (al-idafah) into four main types, as reported by the commentator. It begins by quoting Ibn Sina's statement that relations are almost confined to divisions: in mu'adalat (equivalence), such as dominant, overpowering, preventer; in action and passion, such as cutting and breaking; in muhakat (representation), such as knowledge and report; and in ittihad (union), such as adjacency, similarity, likeness, and equality. The commentator then notes that in the Eastern Investigations the classification is slightly different: divisions of mu'adalat, those by increase (ziyadah), those by action and passion and their source from power, and those by representation. The increase type is further subdivided into quantity (e.g., little/many) and power (e.g., dominant, overpowering, preventer). The action/passion type includes father/son, cutter/cut. The representation type includes knowledge/known, sense/sensed, because knowledge represents the form of the known and sense represents the form of the sensed. The commentator emphasizes that this classification is not precisely delimitable—the phrase 'almost' indicates approximate enumeration. He then provides extensive commentary on each term, explaining mu'adalat as being equal or counterpart, masdar as either emanation or beginning of emanation, muhakat as narration or imitation, and the examples. The passage concludes with a note that the text replaced 'mu'adalat' with 'ziyadah' to match transmissions, and that report can stand in for sense since it also represents the reported state. The overall teaching is that relation divides into categories, but the enumeration is tentative.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ibn Sina's classification of relations is intended as an exhaustive and precise division.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that relations are 'almost confined' (تكاد تنحصر) and that the enumeration is not precisely delimitable, showing it is approximate.

Checkpoint
Which type of relation does the example 'knowledge and the known' belong to according to Ibn Sina's classification?
A) Mu'adalat (equivalence)
B) Fi'l and infi'al (action and passion)
C) Muhakat (representation)
D) Ittihad (union)

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina classifies relations into four approximate types—equivalence, action/passion, representation, and union/increase—but acknowledges that this enumeration is not exhaustive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Ibn Sina acknowledges his classification is approximate. In your own life, when you categorize people (friend, stranger, enemy), do you remember that your labels are incomplete and fallible? How often do you treat your categories as if they were exhaustive, and what harm does that cause?
?
Consider the relation of representation: your knowledge of Allah is a mere representation, not the essence. Does that humble your certainty about what you 'know' of Him? How would your worship change if you truly felt that gap between your mental image and the reality?
?
The relation of ‘father and son’ is action/passion in Ibn Sina's scheme. You are a son or daughter—do you recognize that your very existence is a passive reception from your parents, and ultimately from Allah? How does that shape your gratitude and dependence?

Logic and Technical Terms

Relation Applies to All Categories
Al-Sanūsī enumerates categories and gives relational examples for each.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the category of relation can be predicated of all ten Aristotelian categories, including the Necessary Existent, and see examples for each.

Core Idea
Relation is a universal accident that pervades every category of existence.

In this lesson, we examine the fourth point of the discussion on relation (al-iḍāfa). The author demonstrates that relation is not confined to a specific category but applies to all, even to God as 'the First'. By working through the examples, you will grasp the universality of relation in classical Islamic metaphysics.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the translation. Notice the list of categories and examples.
The author uses a very compact style; each category is given one or two relational pairs.
2
Identify the categories mentioned
Go through each category: substance, quantity, quality, relation, 'where', time, position, possession, action, passion. Note the relational examples given for each.
3
Note the divine example
Observe that relation even applies to the Necessary Existent (God) as 'the First'. This is a key point.
This shows that relation is not limited to contingent beings.
4
Connect to the universality of relation
Reflect on why this matters: relation is a transcendental accident that can be found in any category, making it a fundamental concept in metaphysics.
This prepares you for later discussions on divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : الإضافة قد تعرض للمقولات كلها بل للواجب تعالى أيضاً كالأول (فالجوهر كالأب والابن والكم كالصغير والكبير) من المقادير ( والقليل والكثير من الأعداد ) والكيف كالأحر والأبرد والمضاف كالأقرب والأبعد والابن كالأعلى والأسفل، ومتى كالأقدم والأحدث، والوضع كالأشد انحناء وانتصاباً والملك كالأكسى والأعرى والفعل كالأقطع، والانفعال كالاشد تسخناً . الخامس : قد يكون لها من الطرفين اسم ) أي يكون لها باعتبار كل واحد من طرفيهما اسم مفرد مخصوص بذلك الطرف كالأبوة والبنوة ) أو من أحدهما ) فقط كالمبدئية ( أولا ) يكون لها اسم مخصوص بشيء من طرفيها كالأخوة ( السادس : قد يوضع لها ولموضوعها معاً (اسم فيدل ذلك الاسم (عليها بالتضمن) سواء كان اسما مشتقا كالعالم أو غير مشتق كالجناح .
قوله : ( كالمبدئية) أي بالقياس إلى ذي المبدأ لا بالنسبة إلى المنتهى لانتفاء الإضافة بينهما ولكون الاسم المفرد لكل منهما .
Fourth: Relation may occur in all categories, even in the Necessary Exalted, like the first. Thus substance like father and son, quantity like the small and the large among measures, and the few and the many among numbers, quality like the hotter and the colder, relation like the nearer and the farther, the son like the higher and the lower, time like the earlier and the later, position like the more bent and more upright, possession like the more dressed and the more naked, action like the cutter, passion like the more heated. Fifth: It may have from both sides a name—i.e., it may have, with respect to each of its two sides, a single term specific to that side, like fatherhood and sonship; or from one side only, like principality (al-mubda'iyyah); or it may not have a name specific to either side, like brotherhood. Sixth: A name may be placed for it and its subject together, so that the name indicates the relation by implication (tadammun), whether the name is derived like 'knower' or non-derived like 'wing'. His saying: (like principality) i.e., in relation to the possessor of the principle, not in relation to the endpoint, since there is no mutual relation between the principle and the endpoint, and because each has a single term.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage covers two related topics: the applicability of relation to all categories, and the naming conventions for relations. The fourth point states that relation can occur in all ten Aristotelian categories (substance, quantity, quality, etc.) and even in the Necessary Exalted (God) as 'the First'. Examples are given for each category: in substance, relation occurs as father and son; in quantity, as small/large (measures) and few/many (numbers); in quality, as hotter/colder; in relation itself, as nearer/farther; in the 'where' category (here 'ibn' is used? Actually 'al-ibn' is an example of relative position? The text says 'wal-ibn ka'l-a'la wal-asfal' which seems to be a category label 'where'? Possibly 'ayn' misread? But the commentary does not elaborate, so we follow the text); in time, as earlier/later; in position, as more bent/upright; in possession, as more dressed/more naked; in action, as cutter; in passion, as more heated. This demonstrates that relation is a transcendental accident pervading all categories. The fifth point discusses the names of relations: some relations have a special name for each side (e.g., fatherhood and sonship), some have a name only from one side (e.g., principality, which is only named from the principle side, not from the endpoint because there is no relation back?), and some have no specific name for either side (e.g., brotherhood, which is a single term covering both). The sixth point states that sometimes a name is coined for the relation together with its subject, so that the name implies the relation by tadammun (implication), whether the name is derived (like 'alim from 'ilm) or non-derived (like janah, wing). The commentator's note on 'kalmubda'iyyah' clarifies that the relation is considered only with respect to the principle, not the endpoint, because the principle and endpoint are not correlative (they are not in a mutual relation). The teaching here is that relation is universal across categories and that its naming follows patterns reflecting which sides are explicitly named.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Relation cannot be attributed to God because He is not a category.
Correction: The text explicitly states that relation applies even to the Necessary Exalted, e.g., 'the First'.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which category includes 'father and son'?
A) Substance
B) Quantity
C) Quality
D) Relation

Key Takeaway
Relation is a universal accident applicable to all categories, including the Necessary Existent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you describe Allah as 'the First', do you truly grasp that He alone is absolute while everything else is relative? In what area of your life are you still treating something relative as if it were first in your heart?
?
The passage shows that 'possession' includes being 'more dressed or more naked.' Think about what you own: Do your possessions define you more than your relationship with Allah? What would it mean for you to hold them lightly?
?
Relation pervades every category of existence, yet we often forget that everything is connected to and dependent on Allah. Where in your life have you ignored this dependence, acting as if you are self-sufficient? How will you consciously remember Him as the First today?

Naming Conventions for Relations
Al-Sanūsī classifies relations by naming patterns with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three patterns of naming relations: names from both sides, one side, or no specific name.

Core Idea
Relations are named according to whether both sides, one side, or neither side has a dedicated term.

The fifth point of the text classifies relations based on their naming. Some relations have distinct names for each side (like fatherhood and sonship), some only from one side (like principality), and some have no specific name for either side (like brotherhood). This lesson unpacks each pattern.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation, focusing on the three cases described.
Notice the examples: fatherhood/sonship, principality, brotherhood.
2
Identify the three categories of naming
List them: (1) name from both sides, (2) name from one side, (3) no name from either side.
3
Reflect on the examples
For each case, understand why the example fits. Pay special attention to 'principality' (al-mubda'iyyah) which is named only from the principle side.
The commentator adds that principality is not mutual; there is no relation back from the endpoint.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

But the commentary does not elaborate, so we follow the text); in time, as earlier/later; in position, as more bent/upright; in possession, as more dressed/more naked; in action, as cutter; in passion, as more heated. How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your relationship with Allah includes terms like 'Lord' and 'Servant'. According to Al-Sanūsī's classification, is this relation named from both sides? How does the asymmetry deepen your awareness of Allah's absolute sovereignty and your complete dependence? What changes in your daily life if you truly internalize that you have no 'name' or claim over Allah?
?
Think of a relationship in your life where you have a specific role (e.g., parent, teacher) but the other person has no specific title for their role toward you. How does this naming asymmetry affect your sense of responsibility and humility? Can you see a reflection of the divine principle that Allah alone is the ultimate source, while creation merely receives?
?
The term 'brotherhood' lacks distinct names for each side. In your own experience of brotherhood in faith, are you more focused on what you receive or what you give? How does realizing that brotherhood is a shared, non-directional relation challenge your ego and push you toward selfless unity with fellow believers?
