Names Implying Relation by Tadammun
Al-Sanūsī explains names that imply relation and subject together, and the commentator clarifies principality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how some names indicate both a subject and its relation by implication (tadammun), and the clarification on principality.

Core Idea
A name can imply a relation and its subject together, whether derived or non-derived.

The sixth point and the commentator's note discuss another naming phenomenon: a single term that implies both the relation and its subject, like 'knower' (implying knowledge and the knower). The commentator clarifies the case of principality to emphasise unidirectional relation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and commentator's note
Read the sixth point and the commentator's clarification together. The passage is short but packed.
Focus on the concept of tadammun (implication).
2
Understand tadammun
A name like 'knower' contains both the subject (the knower) and the relation (knowledge) by implication. The same for 'wing' which implies a relation to a body.
Derived names (ishtiqāq) and non-derived names both can have this property.
3
Examine the principality clarification
The commentator explains that principality is only named from the principle side because the relation is not mutual.
This reinforces the earlier lesson on unidirectional naming.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : الإضافة قد تعرض للمقولات كلها بل للواجب تعالى أيضاً كالأول (فالجوهر كالأب والابن والكم كالصغير والكبير) من المقادير ( والقليل والكثير من الأعداد ) والكيف كالأحر والأبرد والمضاف كالأقرب والأبعد والابن كالأعلى والأسفل، ومتى كالأقدم والأحدث، والوضع كالأشد انحناء وانتصاباً والملك كالأكسى والأعرى والفعل كالأقطع، والانفعال كالاشد تسخناً . الخامس : قد يكون لها من الطرفين اسم ) أي يكون لها باعتبار كل واحد من طرفيهما اسم مفرد مخصوص بذلك الطرف كالأبوة والبنوة ) أو من أحدهما ) فقط كالمبدئية ( أولا ) يكون لها اسم مخصوص بشيء من طرفيها كالأخوة ( السادس : قد يوضع لها ولموضوعها معاً (اسم فيدل ذلك الاسم (عليها بالتضمن) سواء كان اسما مشتقا كالعالم أو غير مشتق كالجناح .
قوله : ( كالمبدئية) أي بالقياس إلى ذي المبدأ لا بالنسبة إلى المنتهى لانتفاء الإضافة بينهما ولكون الاسم المفرد لكل منهما .
Fourth: Relation may occur in all categories, even in the Necessary Exalted, like the first. Thus substance like father and son, quantity like the small and the large among measures, and the few and the many among numbers, quality like the hotter and the colder, relation like the nearer and the farther, the son like the higher and the lower, time like the earlier and the later, position like the more bent and more upright, possession like the more dressed and the more naked, action like the cutter, passion like the more heated. Fifth: It may have from both sides a name—i.e., it may have, with respect to each of its two sides, a single term specific to that side, like fatherhood and sonship; or from one side only, like principality (al-mubda'iyyah); or it may not have a name specific to either side, like brotherhood. Sixth: A name may be placed for it and its subject together, so that the name indicates the relation by implication (tadammun), whether the name is derived like 'knower' or non-derived like 'wing'. His saying: (like principality) i.e., in relation to the possessor of the principle, not in relation to the endpoint, since there is no mutual relation between the principle and the endpoint, and because each has a single term.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage covers two related topics: the applicability of relation to all categories, and the naming conventions for relations. The fourth point states that relation can occur in all ten Aristotelian categories (substance, quantity, quality, etc.) and even in the Necessary Exalted (God) as 'the First'. Examples are given for each category: in substance, relation occurs as father and son; in quantity, as small/large (measures) and few/many (numbers); in quality, as hotter/colder; in relation itself, as nearer/farther; in the 'where' category (here 'ibn' is used? Actually 'al-ibn' is an example of relative position? The text says 'wal-ibn ka'l-a'la wal-asfal' which seems to be a category label 'where'? Possibly 'ayn' misread? But the commentary does not elaborate, so we follow the text); in time, as earlier/later; in position, as more bent/upright; in possession, as more dressed/more naked; in action, as cutter; in passion, as more heated. This demonstrates that relation is a transcendental accident pervading all categories. The fifth point discusses the names of relations: some relations have a special name for each side (e.g., fatherhood and sonship), some have a name only from one side (e.g., principality, which is only named from the principle side, not from the endpoint because there is no relation back?), and some have no specific name for either side (e.g., brotherhood, which is a single term covering both). The sixth point states that sometimes a name is coined for the relation together with its subject, so that the name implies the relation by tadammun (implication), whether the name is derived (like 'alim from 'ilm) or non-derived (like janah, wing). The commentator's note on 'kalmubda'iyyah' clarifies that the relation is considered only with respect to the principle, not the endpoint, because the principle and endpoint are not correlative (they are not in a mutual relation). The teaching here is that relation is universal across categories and that its naming follows patterns reflecting which sides are explicitly named.

Correction: The text explicitly gives 'wing' as a non-derived name that implies relation by tadammun.

The First Type of Priority: Priority by Causality
Priority is of five types; the first is priority by causality with example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of priority by causality and its example in the philosophers' classification of priority.

Core Idea
Causal priority is the priority of a cause over its effect.

The philosophers classify priority into five types. The first type, priority by causality, is essential for understanding causal relationships. This passage defines it through the illuminating-light example and includes a clarification on principality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the definition and example.
2
Identify the core idea
Understand that priority by causality is defined as the cause being prior to its effect. The illuminating is the cause, light is the effect.
3
Analyze the commentator's clarification
Note how the commentator explains the meaning of 'priority of the illuminating' and the remark on 'principality' as relative to the principle, not the endpoint.
The principality remark may be a side note from a previous discussion.
4
Apply the concept
Reflect on how causal priority differs from temporal priority; it is ontological, not necessarily temporal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الخامس : قول الحكماء في أقسام المضاف ] ( ومن أقسام المضاف التقدم والتأخر قال الحكماء : التقدم على خمسة أوجه الأول : ( التقدم ( بالعلية كتقدم المضيء على الضوء) الفائض منه (و) تقدم
قوله : ( كتقدم المضيء ) أي تقدم الشيء الموجب لوجود شيء بحيث لا يتخلف عنه أو
قوله : ( كالمبدئية) أي بالنسبة إلى ذي المبدأ لا بالنسبة إلى المنتهى، إذ لا تضايف بين المبدأ والمنتهى كما سلف
Fifth Objective: The statement of the philosophers concerning the divisions of the relative [al-mudaf]. Among the divisions of the relative are priority and posteriority. The philosophers said: priority is of five aspects. The first: priority by causality, like the priority of the illuminating over the light emanating from it, and... His saying: (like the priority of the illuminating) i.e., the priority of a thing that necessitates the existence of something such that it is not separate from it, or... His saying: (like principality) i.e., in relation to the possessor of the principle, not in relation to the endpoint, since there is no mutual relation (tadaayuf) between the principle and the endpoint, as has preceded.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage introduces a new topic: priority (taqaddum) and posteriority (ta'akhkhur) as divisions of the relative (al-mudaf). The philosophers state that priority has five aspects, and the first aspect is priority by causality (bi'l-'illiyyah). The example given is the priority of the illuminating (al-mudi') over the light (al-daw') that emanates from it. The illuminating is the cause and the light is the effect. The commentator explains 'priority of the illuminating' as the priority of a thing that necessitates the existence of another thing in such a way that the latter is not separate from it. The passage also includes a comment on 'kalmubda'iyyah' (like principality) which is likely a carryover from the previous discussion; it explains that principality is considered only relative to the principle, not the endpoint, because the principle and endpoint are not correlative (no tadaayuf). This brief lesson focuses on the definition of causal priority with a concrete example, as the first of five types of priority.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Priority always means temporal priority.
Correction: Causal priority is ontological, not necessarily temporal. The cause is prior in existence, not time.

Key Takeaway
Priority by causality means the cause is prior to its effect, as exemplified by the illuminating and light.

When you feel your faith weakening, do you return to the cause, or do you neglect it?
?
The philosophers say the cause is prior to the effect. In your daily life, what is the true cause of your happiness, your peace, your success? Do you attribute these effects to their real cause—Allah—or do you forget Him and attribute them to secondary causes? How can you make the remembrance of the First Cause central to your day?
?
This lesson teaches that priority by causality is not temporal but ontological. How does this understanding affect your view of Allah's relationship to creation? Are you living as if Allah is the continual cause of your existence at every moment, or do you take His existence for granted? What would change if you truly felt that you are the light and He is the illuminating?

The Fourth Division of al-Iḍāfa: Applying to All Categories
Enumerates fourth division of al-iḍāfa with examples from ten categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-iḍāfa (the relative) can apply to all ten categories of being, including the Necessary Existent, with specific examples.

Core Idea
Al-iḍāfa permeates every category, even the divine attributes.

The commentator expands on the classification of the relative by showing its universal scope. Even the Necessary Existent (God) can be described in relative terms. This lesson unpacks the fourth division with examples from all Aristotelian categories.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the fourth division
Read the Arabic passage from 'الرابع' to the end of examples, then the English translation. Notice the variety of categories listed.
2
Identify the categories mentioned
List the ten categories (substance, quantity, quality, etc.) and how a relative term appears in each.
The text explicitly says 'even for the Necessary Exalted'.
3
Reflect on the significance of including the Necessary
Consider why the commentator emphasizes that al-iḍāfa applies to God. This shows the relational aspect of some divine attributes.
This counters a possible misconception that God is beyond any relation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بدل في عبارة الكتاب لفظ المعادلة بلفظ الزيادة لتطابق المنقولان بحسب المعنى، إذ يكون حينئذ قوله : وفي الاتحاد قائماً مقام المعادلة وإما وقوع الخبر موقع الحس فلا بأس به، لأن الخبر أيضاً حكاية هيئة المخبر عنه ( الرابع : الإضافة قد تعرض للمقولات كلها بل للواجب تعالى أيضاً كالأول (فالجوهر كالأب والابن والكم كالصغير والكبير) من المقادير ( والقليل والكثير من الأعداد ) والكيف كالأحر والأبرد والمضاف كالأقرب والأبعد والابن كالأعلى والأسفل، ومتى كالأقدم والأحدث، والوضع كالأشد انحناء وانتصاباً والملك كالأكسى والأعرى والفعل كالأقطع، والانفعال كالاشد تسخناً . الخامس : قد يكون لها من الطرفين اسم ) أي يكون لها باعتبار كل واحد من طرفيهما اسم مفرد مخصوص بذلك الطرف كالأبوة والبنوة ) أو من أحدهما ) فقط كالمبدئية ( أولا ) يكون لها اسم مخصوص بشيء من طرفيها كالأخوة ( السادس : قد يوضع لها ولموضوعها معاً (اسم فيدل ذلك الاسم (عليها بالتضمن) سواء كان اسما مشتقا كالعالم أو غير مشتق كالجناح .
He replaced in the expression of the book the word 'al-mu'ādala' with the word 'al-ziyāda' for correspondence of the two transmitted [texts] in meaning, for then his statement: 'and in union' would stand in place of 'al-mu'ādala'; and if the happening of the report in place of sensation is permissible, because the report is also a narration of the state of the reported about. (The fourth: The relative (al-iḍāfa) may occur in all categories, even for the Necessary Exalted, like the first. Thus substance like father and son, quantity like small and large from magnitudes, and few and many from numbers, quality like hotter and colder, relative like nearer and farther, position like higher and lower, time like older and newer, posture like more bent and straight, possession like more clothed and naked, action like cutting, passion like being more heated. The fifth: It may have a name from both sides, i.e., it may have, with respect to each one of its two sides, a singular name specific to that side, like fatherhood and sonship, or from one side only, like 'mubda'iyya', or it may not have a name specific to either side, like brotherhood. The sixth: A name may be placed for it and its subject together, so that name indicates it by inclusion (bi-l-tadamun), whether it is a derived name like 'ālim or non-derived like janāḥ.)
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the text's classification of the relative. It begins with a textual note: the commentator states that the author replaced the word 'al-mu'ādala' with 'al-ziyāda' to align the transmitted texts. Then it enumerates three further divisions of the relative (al-iḍāfa). The fourth division states that the relative can apply to all categories of being, including the Necessary Existent (i.e., God), and gives examples for each of Aristotle's ten categories: substance (e.g., father-son), quantity (small-large, few-many), quality (hotter-colder), etc. The fifth division discusses the naming of the relative: it may have a special name for each side (like fatherhood and sonship), or for only one side (like 'mubda'iyya' for the source side), or no special name at all (like brotherhood). The sixth division notes that a single name may denote both the relation and its subject by way of inclusion (tadamun), whether the name is derived (like 'ālim) or not (like 'wing'). This classification shows the extensive scope of the relative, reaching even to divine attributes, and the subtlety of its linguistic expression.

المقولة
الواجب

Misconception: One might think the relative only applies to created things, not to God.
Correction: The text explicitly states that al-iḍāfa applies even to the Necessary Exalted, showing that relational attributes can be affirmed of God in a way that does not imply imperfection.

The Fifth Division of al-Iḍāfa: Naming from the Sides
Classifies the fifth division by naming possibilities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three possibilities for naming al-iḍāfa: a special name for each side, for one side only, or no special name at all.

Core Idea
Al-iḍāfa can be named from both sides, one side, or neither side.

The commentator now turns to the linguistic aspect of the relative: how it is named. This lesson explores the fifth division, which classifies the relative based on whether there is a specific name for one or both of the relata.

Brotherhood does not have distinct words for each sibling.

The Sixth Division of al-Iḍāfa: Names of Inclusion
Describes sixth division: names denoting relation and subject by inclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a single name can denote both the relation and its subject by way of inclusion (tadamun).

Core Idea
A name can denote the relation and its subject together by inclusion.

The final division discussed here concerns names that indicate the relative and its relata simultaneously. These names, whether derived or not, signify the relation by way of inclusion (bi-l-tadamun).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the sixth division
Read the Arabic and translation. Focus on the concept of 'inclusion' (tadamun).
2
Understand 'inclusion'
A term like 'ālim (knower) implies a relation of knowledge and the subject who knows. The relation is included in the meaning.
3
Compare derived vs. non-derived names
The text gives example of derived ('ālim) and non-derived (janāḥ, wing). Both can indicate the relation by inclusion.

بدل في عبارة الكتاب لفظ المعادلة بلفظ الزيادة لتطابق المنقولان بحسب المعنى، إذ يكون حينئذ قوله : وفي الاتحاد قائماً مقام المعادلة وإما وقوع الخبر موقع الحس فلا بأس به، لأن الخبر أيضاً حكاية هيئة المخبر عنه ( الرابع : الإضافة قد تعرض للمقولات كلها بل للواجب تعالى أيضاً كالأول (فالجوهر كالأب والابن والكم كالصغير والكبير) من المقادير ( والقليل والكثير من الأعداد ) والكيف كالأحر والأبرد والمضاف كالأقرب والأبعد والابن كالأعلى والأسفل، ومتى كالأقدم والأحدث، والوضع كالأشد انحناء وانتصاباً والملك كالأكسى والأعرى والفعل كالأقطع، والانفعال كالاشد تسخناً . الخامس : قد يكون لها من الطرفين اسم ) أي يكون لها باعتبار كل واحد من طرفيهما اسم مفرد مخصوص بذلك الطرف كالأبوة والبنوة ) أو من أحدهما ) فقط كالمبدئية ( أولا ) يكون لها اسم مخصوص بشيء من طرفيها كالأخوة ( السادس : قد يوضع لها ولموضوعها معاً (اسم فيدل ذلك الاسم (عليها بالتضمن) سواء كان اسما مشتقا كالعالم أو غير مشتق كالجناح .
He replaced in the expression of the book the word 'al-mu'ādala' with the word 'al-ziyāda' for correspondence of the two transmitted [texts] in meaning, for then his statement: 'and in union' would stand in place of 'al-mu'ādala'; and if the happening of the report in place of sensation is permissible, because the report is also a narration of the state of the reported about. (The fourth: The relative (al-iḍāfa) may occur in all categories, even for the Necessary Exalted, like the first. Thus substance like father and son, quantity like small and large from magnitudes, and few and many from numbers, quality like hotter and colder, relative like nearer and farther, position like higher and lower, time like older and newer, posture like more bent and straight, possession like more clothed and naked, action like cutting, passion like being more heated. The fifth: It may have a name from both sides, i.e., it may have, with respect to each one of its two sides, a singular name specific to that side, like fatherhood and sonship, or from one side only, like 'mubda'iyya', or it may not have a name specific to either side, like brotherhood. The sixth: A name may be placed for it and its subject together, so that name indicates it by inclusion (bi-l-tadamun), whether it is a derived name like 'ālim or non-derived like janāḥ.)
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the text's classification of the relative. It begins with a textual note: the commentator states that the author replaced the word 'al-mu'ādala' with 'al-ziyāda' to align the transmitted texts. Then it enumerates three further divisions of the relative (al-iḍāfa). The fourth division states that the relative can apply to all categories of being, including the Necessary Existent (i.e., God), and gives examples for each of Aristotle's ten categories: substance (e.g., father-son), quantity (small-large, few-many), quality (hotter-colder), etc. The fifth division discusses the naming of the relative: it may have a special name for each side (like fatherhood and sonship), or for only one side (like 'mubda'iyya' for the source side), or no special name at all (like brotherhood). The sixth division notes that a single name may denote both the relation and its subject by way of inclusion (tadamun), whether the name is derived (like 'ālim) or not (like 'wing'). This classification shows the extensive scope of the relative, reaching even to divine attributes, and the subtlety of its linguistic expression.

Precedence by Essence: The Second Type of Priority
Definition and explanation of essential priority using the example of one and two, with contrast to causal priority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the concept of essential priority (t.qaddum bi-dhāt) and how it differs from causal priority and why it is specific to the part-whole relationship.

Core Idea
Essential priority is a logical dependence of the whole on its parts, not based on time or existence.

The commentator now turns to the second type of priority: priority by essence. Unlike the first type, which concerns existence, this type is about the intrinsic nature of things. Using the example of one and two, he shows how the whole depends on its parts in its very essence. This lesson unpacks that idea and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the example of one and two used to illustrate essential priority.
This example is key to understanding the concept.
2
Identify the key distinctions
Note how the commentator contrasts essential priority with causal priority. Understand why he says essential priority is specific to part-whole relationships.
Also note the reference to al-Shifā' at the end.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider why essential priority is called natural priority. Think about how this concept applies in kalam arguments about the nature of things.
This helps ground the abstract concept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : التقدم بالذات كتقدم الواحد على الاثنين فإنه لا تعقل ذات الاثنين وهو ذات هذا الواحد وذاك الواحد معاً ولا يتم له ذات إلا بذاتهما سواء فرضنا لهما وجوداً أم لا، بل ذلك حكم له باعتبار ذاته وحقيقته من حيث هي بخلاف الأول فإنه حكم باعتبار الوجود لا باعتبار الماهية في نفسها، وقد ظهر مما ذكره أن التقدم الذاتي المسمى بالتقدم الطبيعي مخصوص بجزء الشيء مقيساً إلى كله دون سائر علله الناقصة، والمشهور في كتب القوم أن المحتاج إليه إن كفي في وجود المحتاج كان هو الفاعل التام في إيجاده فقط، أو بانضمام أمر آخر في الشفاء ما حاصله : إذا كان وجود الثاني من الأول على تجويز أن يكون الأول منهما لزم أن يكون علة لوجوب وجود الثاني، فإن الأول يكون متقدماً لوجود هذا الثاني
The second: priority by essence, like the priority of the one over the two. For the essence of the two is not conceived, and it is the essence of this one and that one together, and its essence is not realized except by their essences, whether we assume existence for them or not. Rather, that is a judgment for it in consideration of its essence and reality in themselves, unlike the first, for it is a judgment in consideration of existence, not of quiddity in itself. And it has become apparent from what he mentioned that essential priority, called natural priority, is specific to the part of a thing measured to its whole, to the exclusion of its other incomplete causes. And the well-known in the books of the people is that that which is needed, if it suffices in the existence of the needy, then it is the perfect agent in its bringing into existence only, or with the joining of another matter. In al-Shifā', the gist of it is: if the existence of the second is from the first, upon the possibility that the first is from them, then it must be a cause for the necessity of the existence of the second, for the first is prior to the existence of this second.
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage defines and explains the second type of priority: priority by essence (t.qaddum bi-dhāt). The example given is the priority of the one over the two: the number two is composed of two ones, and its essence depends on the essences of the two ones. This dependency is not about existence but about essence itself; even if the ones and two have no existence, the two is still dependent on the ones in its quiddity. This is contrasted with the first type (causal priority), which concerns existence. The commentator then notes that this essential priority, also called natural priority (t.qaddum ṭabī'ī), is specific to the part relative to its whole, not to other incomplete causes. Finally, he references a principle from Ibn Sina's al-Shifā' about the cause necessitating the existence of the effect: if the existence of the second is from the first and the first is sufficient, it is a perfect cause; otherwise it requires something else. This reinforces the idea that essential priority is intrinsic to the nature of things.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think essential priority is the same as temporal priority.
Correction: Essential priority is based on logical dependence in essence, not in time or existence.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Essential priority is a logical precedence based on the dependency of the whole on its parts in their very quiddity, not on existence or time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Temporal Priority in Causal Sequences
Al-Jurjani defines temporal priority with the example of Moses and Jesus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what temporal priority is, how it differs from causal and essential priority, and why it is considered a property of time itself.

Core Idea
Temporal priority is about time, not causation or essence.

We have already seen two types of priority: causal (based on dependence) and essential (based on essence). Now Al-Jurjani introduces a third: temporal priority. Using the clear example of Moses preceding Jesus, he shows that this priority belongs to time, not to the entities themselves. Let's explore how this works and why it matters.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how Al-Jurjani defines temporal priority with an example.
The passage is short but dense. Read it twice.
2
Identify the definition and example
Find the sentence that gives the meaning of temporal priority. Identify the example of Moses and Jesus and how it illustrates the concept.
The example is: Moses existed in a time that passed, then Jesus existed in a later time.
3
Understand the key insight
Notice Al-Jurjani says priority here is a quality of time itself, not of the essence or accidents of Moses. Ask yourself: Why is this distinction important?
Temporal priority does not imply causation or inherent superiority.
4
Contrast with the first two types
Read the final sentence where Al-Jurjani distinguishes temporal priority from causal and essential priority. Understand each difference.
Causal priority is about existence and need; essential priority is about quiddity; temporal priority is about time.
5
Reflect and self-test
Explain in your own words why the priority of Moses over Jesus is temporal, not causal or essential. Then answer the checkpoint question.
This solidifies your understanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(الثالث: التقدم بالزمان كتقدم موسى على عيسى عليهما السلام فإنه ليس لذات موسى ولا شيء من عوارضها إلا الزمان، فمعناه أن موسى وجد في زمان ثم انقضى ذلك الزمان ) وجاء زمان آخر ( وجد فيه عيسى) فالتقدم هاهنا صفة للزمان أولاً وبالذات ( ومغايرته للأولين بينة ) إذ ليس شيء منهما راجعاً إلى الزمان بل الأول باعتبار الوجود والاحتياج إليه، والثاني باعتبار ذات الشيء وماهيته
(The third: priority by time, like the priority of Moses over Jesus, peace be upon them. For it is not due to the essence of Moses nor to any of its accidents except time. So its meaning is that Moses existed in a time, then that time passed away, and another time came in which Jesus existed. So priority here is a quality of time firstly and intrinsically. And its difference from the first two is clear, for neither of them is referable to time; rather the first is by virtue of existence and need for it, and the second is by virtue of the thing's essence and quiddity.)
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage defines the third type of priority: temporal priority (al-taqaddum bi al-zaman). Using the example of Moses preceding Jesus, the author explains that the priority is not due to the essence of Moses or any of its attributes except time itself. The meaning is that Moses existed in one time, which then passed, and Jesus existed in a subsequent time. Thus, temporal priority is primarily a property of time itself, not of the entities. The author then contrasts this with the first two types: the first (causal) is based on existence and dependence, and the second (essential) is based on a thing's essence and quiddity, neither of which involves time directly.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Temporal priority is the same as causal priority.
Correction: Causal priority involves one thing depending on another for existence, whereas temporal priority merely involves succession in time without causation.

Key Takeaway
Temporal priority is primarily a quality of time, not of the entities that exist within time.

Does it challenge you to trust His timing more, even when you feel 'behind' or 'ahead' in life? What is one area where you need to stop measuring yourself by time and start trusting His purpose?

Priority by Honor, Rank, and Temporal Parts
Al-Jurjani enumerates three types of priority with sub-classifications and a critique.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions and distinctions of priority by honor, rank, and temporal parts, including the mutakallimun's addition and the conventional nature of honor priority.

Core Idea
Priority by honor is conventional and reducible to rank; temporal priority is unique among types.

Al-Jurjani continues his classification of priority by presenting the fourth, fifth, and sixth types. Priority by honor is illustrated with the example of Abu Bakr and Umar, while priority by rank is shown to be either intellectual or conventional. Finally, the mutakallimun add a sixth type: temporal priority, which differs from causal and essential priority. The accompanying commentary clarifies that priority by honor is not truly independent but reduces to sensory rank-priority.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types
Read the passage and note the fourth (honor), fifth (rank), and sixth (temporal parts) types of priority. Observe how each is defined and exemplified.
The example for honor is the precedence of Abu Bakr over Umar.
2
Understand the subdivisions of rank
Notice that priority by rank is divided into intellectual and conventional. The intellectual type is fixed by reason (e.g., genera), while the conventional type depends on a chosen starting point (e.g., mosque rows).
Rank priority can reverse if the principle changes.
3
Examine the mutakallimun's addition and the commentary
The mutakallimun add a sixth type: priority of parts of time (e.g., yesterday over today). The commentary argues that priority by honor is conventional and reduces to rank, so it may not be an independent division. Reflect on why this matters for classification.
The commentary critiques the independence of honor priority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع: التقدم بالشرف كما لأبي بكر على عمر رضي الله عنهما . الخامس : التقدم بالرتبة بأن يكون المتقدم أقرب إلى مبدأ معين والترتب إما عقلي كما في الأجناس المترتبة على سبيل التصاعد والأنواع الإضافية المترتبة على سبيل التنازل فإن كل واحد من هذه الأمور المترتبة واقع في مرتبة يحكم العقل باستحالة وقوعه في غيرها، أو وضعي وهو أن يكفي وقوع المتقدم في مرتبة المتأخر كما في صفوف المسجد، ويختلف ذلك أي التقدم الرتبي حيث يصير المتقدم متأخرا والمتأخر متقدماً بما تجعله أنت مبدأ تبتدئ من المحراب فيكون الصف الأول متقدماً على الصف الأخير وقد تبتدئ من الباب فينعكس الحال وقس على ذلك حال الأجناس فإنك إذا جعلت الجوهر مبدأ كان الجسم متقدماً على الحيوان وإن جعلت الإنسان مبدأ فبالعكس . وقال المتكلمون : هاهنا نوع آخر من التقدم مغاير للوجوه الخمسة المتقدمة كما لأجزاء الزمان بعضها على بعض مثل تقدم الأمس على اليوم واليوم على الغد فإنه ليس تقدماً بالعلية ولا بالذات لعدم الاقتران واستحالته بين أجزاء الزمان مع أن المتقدم والمتأخر في هذين النوعين من التقدم يجوز فيما أقسام التقدم، ففي التقدم في الرتبة ظاهر وفي التقدم بالزمان لأن الحاضر الحال بأن يفرض، وفي التقدم بالشرف نفس المعنى والذي بالشرف كالمبدأ المحدود، فإن السابق في باب له ما ليس للثاني وللثاني منه فهو للسابق وزيادة وفي التقدم بالطبع والعلية الوجود فالمتقدم له وجود وإن لم يمكن الثاني، والثاني لا يكون له إلا وقد كان للأول وجود . قوله : الرابع التقدم بالشرف الظاهر أن إطلاق التقدم على هذا المعنى بحسب الاصطلاح إذ لا تقدم هاهنا بحسب اللغة إلا باعتبار أن زيادة الفضل والشرف سبب للتقدم في المجالس غالباً، وبهذا الاعتبار يرجع إلى التقدم بالرتبة الحسي، فلا يكون قسماً برأسه .
The fourth: priority by honor, like that of Abu Bakr over Umar, may God be pleased with them. The fifth: priority by rank, such that the prior is closer to a specific principle. The ordering is either intellectual, as in genera ordered ascendingly and added species ordered descendingly, for each of these ordered things is in a rank that the intellect judges impossible to be in another; or conventional, which is that it suffices for the prior to occur in the rank of the posterior, as in the rows of the mosque. And that (i.e., priority by rank) varies such that the prior becomes posterior and the posterior prior depending on what you take as principle: if you start from the mihrab, the first row is prior to the last, and if you start from the door, the situation reverses. Apply that to genera: if you make substance the principle, then body is prior to animal; if you make man the principle, it is the opposite. The mutakallimun said: there is another type of priority here different from the five preceding types, like the parts of time over each other, such as the priority of yesterday over today and today over tomorrow. For it is not priority by causality nor by essence due to the lack of conjunction and its impossibility among parts of time, even though the prior and posterior in these two types of priority are permissible in terms of the divisions of priority. So in priority by rank it is apparent, and in temporal priority because the present state can be supposed, and in priority by honor the same meaning, and that which is by honor is like the limited principle. For the precedent in a certain respect has something that the second does not have, and the second has from it, so it is for the precedent and more. And in priority by nature and causality, existence: the prior has existence even if the second is impossible, and the second does not occur except after the first has existence. His statement: The fourth, priority by honor: it is apparent that applying 'priority' to this meaning is conventional, for there is no priority here linguistically except insofar as increase in virtue and honor is often a cause for priority in assemblies. With this consideration, it reduces to sensory priority by rank, and thus it is not an independent division.
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage covers the last three types of priority. First, priority by honor (al-taqaddum bi al-sharaf) is exemplified by Abu Bakr's precedence over Umar. Second, priority by rank (al-taqaddum bi al-rutba) is explained as being closer to a principle; it can be intellectual (as in genera and species) or conventional (as in mosque rows). The author notes that rank-based priority can reverse if the starting point changes. Third, the mutakallimun add a sixth type: priority of parts of time over each other (e.g., yesterday over today), which is neither causal nor essential because temporal parts do not coexist. The commentary (qalahu) at the end clarifies that priority by honor is really a conventional term, reducing to sensory rank-priority, and thus may not be a truly independent division.

التقدم بالرتبة
الترتب العقلي
الترتب الوضعي
أجزاء الزمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Priority by honor is an independent and genuine type of priority.
Correction: The commentary explains that priority by honor is conventional and reduces to sensory priority by rank, so it is not an independent division.

Checkpoint
What is the sixth type of priority added by the mutakallimun?
A) Priority by causality
B) Priority by essence
C) Priority of parts of time over each other
D) Priority by honor

Key Takeaway
Priority by honor is conventional and reducible to rank; priority by temporal parts is a distinct type added by the mutakallimun.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

The Five Types of Priority According to the Philosophers
Enumerates and defines the five types of priority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five types of priority as classified by the philosophers, including their definitions, examples, and how they differ from one another.

Core Idea
Priority is not just about time; philosophers recognize five distinct types.

The philosophers (hukama') developed a sophisticated classification of priority (taqaddum) beyond mere temporal precedence. This passage from the text enumerates five types, each with its own basis and example. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for grasping Islamic theological and philosophical discourse on causation, hierarchy, and honor.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Let the author's enumeration and definitions come through.
2
Identify the five types
List the five types of priority as they appear in the passage. Notice the phrasing: 'تقدم بالعلية', 'تقدم بالذات والطبع', etc.
3
Understand each definition and example
For each type, note how it is defined and what example is given. Pay special attention to the distinction between causality and essence/nature.
The first two types are closely related; priority by essence/nature covers incomplete causes.
4
Recognize the non-temporal types
Notice that priority by time is just one type. The other four are non-temporal: causality, essence/nature, honor, and rank.
This corrects the common misconception that priority is always temporal.
5
Reflect on the relativity of rank priority
How does priority by rank depend on the chosen starting point? Consider the example of mosque rows and genera.
This shows that rank priority is conventional and can reverse.

المرصد الخامس - المقصد الخامس : قول الحكماء في أقسام المضاف
متقدماً عليه بالعلية كالمؤثر المستجمع لشرائط التأثير وارتفاع موانعه، وإن لم يكف كان متقدماً عليه بالذات والطبع، وعلى هذا كان التقدم الطبيعي شاملاً للعلل الناقصة كلها وهم يطلقون التقدم الذاتي على القدر المشترك بين التقدم العلي والتقدم الطبيعي وهو الترتب العقلي الناشئ من الاحتياج المصحح لاستعمال الفاء بينهما داخلة على المحتاج
(الثالث: التقدم بالزمان كتقدم موسى على عيسى عليهما السلام فإنه ليس لذات موسى ولا شيء من عوارضها إلا الزمان، فمعناه أن موسى وجد في زمان ثم انقضى ذلك الزمان وجاء زمان آخر وجد فيه عيسى فالتقدم هاهنا صفة للزمان أولاً وبالذات ومغايرته للأولين بينة إذ ليس شيء منهما راجعاً إلى الزمان بل الأول باعتبار الوجود والاحتياج إليه، والثاني باعتبار ذات الشيء وماهيته
(الرابع : التقدم بالشرف كما لأبي بكر على عمر رضي الله عنهما
(الخامس : التقدم بالرتبة بأن يكون المتقدم أقرب إلى مبدأ معين والترتب إما عقلي كما في الأجناس المترتبة على سبيل التصاعد والأنواع الإضافية المترتبة على سبيل التنازل فإن كل واحد من هذه الأمور المترتبة واقع في مرتبة يحكم العقل باستحالة وقوعه في غيرها، أو وضعي وهو أن يكفي وقوع المتقدم في مرتبة المتأخر كما في صفوف المسجد، ويختلف ذلك أي التقدم الرتبي حيث يصير المتقدم متأخرا والمتأخر متقدماً بما تجعله أنت مبدأ تبتدئ من المحراب فيكون الصف الأول متقدماً على الصف الأخير وقد تبتدئ من الباب فينعكس الحال وقس على ذلك حال الأجناس فإنك إذا جعلت الجوهر مبدأ كان الجسم متقدماً على الحيوان وإن جعلت الإنسان مبدأ فبالعكس
The Fifth Observatory - Fifth Intention: The Statement of the Philosophers on the Divisions of the Relative. It is prior to it by causality, like the efficient cause that meets the conditions for effect and the removal of its impediments. If it is not sufficient, it is prior to it by essence and nature. On this basis, natural priority includes all incomplete causes, and they apply the term 'priority by essence' to the common concept between causal priority and natural priority, which is the intellectual ordering arising from need that justifies the use of fa' (then) between them, inserted upon the needy one. (Third: Priority by time, like the priority of Moses over Jesus, peace be upon them. For it is not due to the essence of Moses nor any of its accidents except time. So its meaning is that Moses existed in a time, then that time passed, and another time came in which Jesus existed. So priority here is an attribute of time firstly and essentially. Its difference from the first two types is clear, for nothing of them pertains to time. Rather, the first is with respect to existence and need of it, and the second is with respect to the essence of the thing and its quiddity. (Fourth: Priority by honor, as that of Abu Bakr over Umar, may God be pleased with them. (Fifth: Priority by rank: that the prior be closer to a specific beginning. The ordering is either intellectual, as in the genera ordered ascendingly and the additional species ordered descendingly, for each of these ordered things is in a rank that reason judges impossible to be in another; or posited, which is that it is sufficient that the prior occur in the rank of the posterior, as in the rows of the mosque. This priority by rank varies, such that the prior becomes posterior and the posterior becomes prior depending on what you make the beginning. If you start from the mihrab, then the first row is prior to the last row. If you start from the door, the situation is reversed. Likewise apply to genera: if you make substance the beginning, then body is prior to animal; if you make man the beginning, the opposite.
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage enumerates and defines the five types of priority (taqaddum) as classified by the philosophers (hukama'). It begins with priority by causality (al-taqaddum bi'l-'illiyya), which is the priority of a complete cause over its effect. If the cause is not sufficient, it is prior by essence and nature (al-taqaddum bi'l-dhat wa'l-tab'), which includes incomplete causes. The text notes that 'priority by essence' is a common term covering both causal and natural priority. The third type is priority by time (al-taqaddum bi'l-zaman), illustrated by the example of Moses and Jesus. The fourth is priority by honor (al-taqaddum bi'l-sharaf), e.g., Abu Bakr over Umar. The fifth is priority by rank (al-taqaddum bi'l-rutba), which can be intellectual (as in genera and species) or conventional (as in mosque rows). The passage explains that rank priority is relative to the chosen starting point. Each type is defined and distinguished from the others.

Key Terms
تقدم بالعلية
تقدم بالذات والطبع
تقدم بالزمان
تقدم بالشرف
تقدم بالرتبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Priority is only about time; earlier things are always prior.
Correction: The philosophers identify four non-temporal types of priority: causality, essence/nature, honor, and rank. Temporal priority is just one type.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers, what type of priority is illustrated by the example of Moses and Jesus?
A) Priority by causality
B) Priority by time
C) Priority by honor
D) Priority by rank

Key Takeaway
The philosophers distinguish five types of priority: causality, essence/nature, time, honor, and rank.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The passage says that priority by rank can change depending on the starting point. What is your starting point in life, and does it lead you to prioritize what matters in the Hereafter?
?

The Sixth Type of Priority: Parts of Time
Discusses the theologians' claim of a sixth type and its reduction to temporal priority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theologians' claim of a sixth type of priority among parts of time and how it is ultimately reduced to temporal priority applied accidentally.

Core Idea
The priority of parts of time is not a new category but temporal priority applied accidentally.

The theologians proposed a sixth type of priority among parts of time, distinct from the five classical types. This passage examines their claim and shows that it is actually temporal priority applied accidentally, preventing infinite regress. Understanding this distinction is key to grasping later debates between philosophers and theologians.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the claim
Read the theologians' claim that there is a sixth type of priority among parts of time, such as yesterday over today. Notice that they assert it is different from the five previous types.
2
Identify why it is not the other types
The passage argues that this priority is not by causality, essence, honor/rank, or time. For each, it gives a reason. Pay attention to the impossibility of coexistence argument.
The impossibility of coexistence is key to negating causality and essence.
3
Understand the answer
The answer given is that this priority is temporal priority applied accidentally. It belongs essentially only to time, and when applied to other things it is accidental, just like division applies to quantity.
The analogy with quantity and division helps clarify.
4
Connect to broader debates
The passage concludes that this issue is foundational for many debates between philosophers and theologians. Consider why this distinction matters for arguments about time and creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال المتكلمون : هاهنا نوع آخر من التقدم مغاير للوجوه الخمسة المتقدمة كما لأجزاء الزمان بعضها على بعض مثل تقدم الأمس على اليوم واليوم على الغد فإنه ليس تقدماً بالعلية ولا بالذات لعدم الاقتران واستحالته بين أجزاء الزمان مع أن المتقدم والمتأخر في هذين النوعين من التقدم يجوز اجتماعهما بل يجب ولا بالشرف والرتبة وهو ظاهر فإن الأمس واليوم مثلاً متشابهان في الفضيلة، وليس بين أجزاء الزمان ترتب عقلي ولا وضعي بل نقول : امتناع الاجتماع كاف لنا في نفي هذه الأربعة ولا بالزمان وإلا لزم التسلسل في الأزمنة بأن يكون كل زمان في زمان آخر وقد أبطلنا ذلك بوجهين في مباحث الزمان وقد يجاب عنه بأن ذلك التقدم الذي بين أجزاء الزمان هو التقدم بالزمان أعني التقدم الذي لا يجامع فيه المتقدم المتأخر وأنه أي هذا التقدم الذي سميناه التقدم الزماني لا يعرض أولاً وبالذات إلا للزمان، فإذا أطلقنا على غيره كان ذلك تقدماً بالعرض لا بالذات كما حققناه في تقدم موسى على عيسى عليهما السلام كما أن القسمة تعرض للكم عروضاً ذاتياً فإذا عرضت لغيره كان بواسطة الكم، وذلك لا للكم كماً آخر، فكذلك هاهنا إذا قلنا لغير الزمان أنه متقدم بهذا التقدم أردنا أن زمانه متقدم ولا يوجب ذلك أن يكون للزمان زمان وقد مر في مباحث الزمان نوع تفصيل لهذا المقام وهذا الذي ذكرناه أعني القسم السادس من التقدم مبني لأبحاث كثيرة بين الطائفتين
And the theologians said: Here is another type of priority different from the five preceding types, like (the priority) of parts of time over each other, such as the priority of yesterday over today and today over tomorrow. For it is not priority by causality nor by essence due to the impossibility of coexistence among parts of time, while the prior and posterior in these two types (of priority) may coexist—rather, they must coexist. Nor is it by honor and rank, as is evident, for yesterday and today, for example, are equal in virtue, and there is no intellectual or posited ordering among parts of time. Rather, we say: the impossibility of coexistence is sufficient for us to negate these four. Nor is it by time, otherwise there would be an infinite regress in times, such that every time would be in another time. And we have refuted that with two proofs in the discussions on time. And it may be answered that this priority that exists among parts of time is priority by time—that is, the priority in which the prior and the posterior do not coexist—and that this priority which we have called temporal priority does not apply firstly and essentially except to time. When we apply it to other than time, it is applied accidentally, not essentially, as we established in the priority of Moses over Jesus, peace be upon them. Just as 'division' applies to quantity essentially, so when it applies to other than quantity, it is through quantity, and that does not make quantity another quantity. Similarly here, when we say of something other than time that it is prior with this priority, we mean that its time is prior, and that does not necessitate that time have a time. And there has passed in the discussions on time a type of detail on this subject. And this which we have mentioned—namely, the sixth type of priority—is founded on many investigations between the two groups.
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage presents the theologians' (mutakallimun) claim that there exists a sixth type of priority—priority among parts of time (taqaddum ajza' al-zaman). They argue that this priority is not reducible to the five types: it is not by causality or essence because parts of time cannot coexist (unlike a cause and effect which can coexist), not by honor or rank because parts of time are equal in virtue and lack ordering, and not by time itself to avoid infinite regress. The answer given is that this priority is indeed temporal priority, but applied accidentally (bi'l-'arad) to things in time; primarily and essentially it applies only to time itself. Just as the property of division belongs essentially to quantity, so temporal priority belongs essentially to time. When we say a non-temporal thing is temporally prior, we mean its time is prior, not that time has a time. The passage concludes that this issue underpins many debates between philosophers and theologians.

تقدم بالعرض
تقدم بالذات
التقدم الزماني

Misconception: One might think the priority of parts of time is a completely separate category of priority, distinct from temporal priority.
Correction: The text argues that it is actually temporal priority applied accidentally; it is not a new essential type.

C) Because it leads to infinite regress

Key Takeaway
The priority of parts of time is not a new type but temporal priority applied accidentally; it does not lead to infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your past mistakes—like yesterday's sin or yesterday's failure—do you treat them as having a kind of essential priority over your present? That is, do you believe your past defines you more than your present choices? How does this lesson's distinction between essential and accidental priority challenge that belief? And what concrete step can you take today to break the tyranny of the past, given that temporal priority is merely accidental?
?
This lesson shows that theologians argued about whether the priority of parts of time is a new category. But consider your own life: how often do you assume that something that came before—whether a tradition, a habit, or a relationship—has inherent authority or superiority simply because it is older? For example, do you give more weight to advice from an elder merely because they are older, rather than evaluating its truth? Based on this lesson, how would you correct that tendency, and what will you do the next time you catch yourself doing it?
?
The passage resolves the issue by saying temporal priority applies essentially to time, not to events. In your daily life, do you sometimes confuse the order of events with true causation? For instance, when two things happen in sequence, do you instinctively assume the first caused the second? How can you use the insight from this lesson to avoid such logical errors in your thinking about your own experiences, especially when attributing blame or credit? Commit to one specific practice this week that will help you distinguish mere sequence from real causality.

Divisions of the Relative and the Qualification 'Of One Type'
Commentary on the philosophers' statement about divisions of the relative from one type.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the qualification 'of one type' is important for the concept of simultaneity and why linguistic debates about the terms are deemed unimportant.

Core Idea
Simultaneity requires more than just causality; it requires a shared type.

In this passage, the commentator addresses two points. First, he dismisses the linguistic classification of terms like 'priority,' 'posteriority,' and 'simultaneity' across the five divisions as unbeneficial. Second, he explains that the qualification 'of one type' is crucial for the realization of simultaneity, because mere causal relation does not guarantee temporal coexistence. This clarifies that the purpose of the qualification is for achieving simultaneity, not for excluding other cases.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation to grasp the overall meaning.
Notice the two distinct parts: the dismissal and the two explanations.
2
Understand the dismissal of linguistic analysis
The commentator says that debating whether the terms 'priority', 'posteriority', and 'simultaneity' are used univocally, equivocally, or metaphorically for the five divisions is not beneficial.
This shows the emphasis on conceptual clarity over verbal classification.
3
Grasp the first explanation of 'of one type'
The qualification is necessary for simultaneity: mere causality (two causes for two individual effects) does not automatically imply coexistence.
Simultaneity requires that the effects share a common type.
4
Grasp the second explanation: not for exclusion
The qualification is not to exclude effects of different types; even if effects are of different types, the principle still applies.
The purpose is to achieve simultaneity, not to limit the scope.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الخامس - المقصد الخامس : قول الحكماء في أقسام المضاف من نوع واحد، وأما بيان أن إطلاق لفظ التقدم والتأخر والمعية على الأقسام الخمسة بالاشتراك المعنوي على سبيل التواطؤ أو التشكيك أو بالاشتراك اللفظي أو بطريق الحقيقة والمجاز فليس فيه كثير فائدة يعتني بشأنها والله أعلم. قوله : ( من نوع واحد) اعتبر هذا القيد لتحقيق المعية فإن مجرد كون العلتين لمعلولين شخصياً لا يوجب كونهما معاً في شيء. قوله: (من نوع واحد) الظاهر أن تقييد المعلولين بكونهما من نوع واحد ليس للاحتراز فإن العلتين لمعلولين من نوعين أيضاً كذلك.
The fifth observation - the fifth aim: The statement of the philosophers on the divisions of the relative from one type. As for the clarification that the usage of the terms 'priority', 'posteriority', and 'simultaneity' for the five divisions is by common meaning on the basis of univocity or gradational ambiguity, or by homonymy, or by way of reality and metaphor, there is not much benefit in it that one should attend to, and God knows best. His statement: (Of one type): this qualification was considered for the realization of simultaneity, for the mere fact that two causes are for two individual effects does not necessitate them being together in anything. His statement: (Of one type): apparently the restriction of the two effects to being of one type is not for exclusion, for the two causes for two effects of two types are also like that.
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a statement about the divisions of the relative (al-mudaf). It first dismisses as unimportant the linguistic discussion of whether the terms priority, posteriority, and simultaneity are applied to the five divisions by common meaning, equivocation, or metaphor. Then it provides two explanations of the qualification 'of one type'. The first explains that this qualification is necessary for simultaneity, because mere causal relation (two causes for two individual effects) does not guarantee that the effects are simultaneous. The second clarifies that the qualification is not meant to exclude cases where effects are of different types, because the same principle applies even if the effects are of two types.

Correction: The passage clarifies that the qualification is for achieving simultaneity, not for exclusion; the same holds for effects of different types.

Defining Substance and Accident by Reference to Earlier Stations
Defines substance and accident by referencing earlier definitions and begins division of substance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Ījī defines substance and accident by referencing earlier definitions from the philosophers and theologians, and how he begins the division of substance.

Core Idea
Substance and accident are defined differently by philosophers and theologians, but both are rooted in earlier definitions.

Before proceeding to the detailed discussion of substances, al-Ījī refreshes the reader's memory by recalling the definitions of substance and accident from the second and third stations. He gives both the philosophical and theological definitions, showing that these terms have been clearly established. This brief introduction sets the stage for the division of substance that follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the definitions
Read the Arabic text and its translation. Notice that al-Ījī does not provide new definitions but refers back to earlier stations. Identify the two definitions of substance (philosopher and theologian) and the two definitions of accident.
The definitions are given in parallel: one for substance and one for accident, each with a philosophical and theological version.
2
Understand the method of reference
Al-Ījī says 'we will not repeat it, relying on your knowledge of it'. This shows that the definitions are already established. Reflect on why the author might choose to remind rather than redefine.
This is a common pedagogical technique in kalam texts.
3
See the beginning of the division
The passage ends with 'And as for its division, the philosophers said: Substance, if it is a state...' This is just the start of a division. Notice that the full division is not given here; it continues in the next parts.
The phrase 'if it is a state' suggests a classification of substance into states (hāl) and other categories.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الموقف الرابع في الجواهر ) وفيه مقدمة ومراصد ( أربعة ( المقدمة : أما تعريفه ) أي تعريف الجوهر ( فقد من التقسيم المذكور في صدر الموقف الثاني وهو أنه ممكن موجود لا في موضوع عند الحكماء وحادث متحيز بالذات عند المتكلمين (و) علمته أيضاً (من تعريف العرض في صدر الموقف الثالث بطريق المقابلة وهو أنه عند الحكيم ماهية إذا وجدت في الأعيان كانت لا في موضوع، وعند المتكلم موجود متحيز بالذات ( فلا نعيده اعتماداً على علمك به ( وأما تقسيمه فقال الحكماء : الجوهر إن كان حالاً) قوله : (ماهية إذا وجدت قد تحقق هذا التعريف بما لا مزيد فيه فتذكر.
The Fourth Station on Substances. It contains an introduction and four observation posts. The introduction: As for its definition (i.e., the definition of substance), it is from the division mentioned at the beginning of the second station, namely that it is a possible existent not in a subject according to the philosophers, and a temporally originated entity inherently occupying space according to the theologians. And you also know from the definition of accident at the beginning of the third station by way of contrast, namely that according to the philosopher it is a quiddity which, when it exists in external entities, is not in a subject, and according to the theologian it is an existent inherently occupying space. So we will not repeat it, relying on your knowledge of it. And as for its division, the philosophers said: Substance, if it is a state... His statement: (A quiddity when it exists) — this definition has been verified in a way that leaves no room for addition, so remember it.
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage is the introduction to the fourth station (al-mawqif al-rabi') on substances (al-jawahir). It first states that the station contains an introduction and four observation posts. Then it provides the definitions of substance and accident by referencing earlier stations. For substance: philosophers define it as a possible existent not in a subject; theologians define it as a temporally originated entity inherently occupying space. For accident: philosophers define it as a quiddity that when it exists in external entities is not in a subject; theologians define it as an existent inherently occupying space. The author says he will not repeat these definitions because the reader already knows them. Then it begins the division of substance with the phrase 'Substance, if it is a state...' (incomplete). The inserted gloss on 'a quiddity when it exists' affirms that the definition of accident is well-established and needs no further elaboration.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The definitions of substance and accident provided here are newly introduced definitions.
Correction: In fact, these definitions are taken from the Second and Third Stations, and the author is merely reminding the reader of them.

Key Takeaway
Substance and accident are defined differently by philosophers and theologians, but these definitions are established from earlier stations and serve as a foundation for the classification of substances.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Etymology of 'Jawahir' (Substances)
Explains the lexical derivation of 'jawahir' for substances.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the lexical origins of the term 'jawahir' and how its technical usage in kalam relates to its linguistic roots.

Core Idea
The term 'jawhar' has a concrete lexical origin before becoming a technical term in kalam.

Before diving into the metaphysical discussions of substances, it is essential to understand the very word 'jawahir' itself. This passage from al-Mawaqif explores the linguistic roots of the term, showing how a word for a stone came to denote the fundamental building blocks of created beings. This grounding in language clarifies why substances are so named in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage in Arabic
Read the Arabic passage once without translation. Notice the key terms: الجوهر, الجهر, القاموس.
Pay attention to the repetition of 'يستخرج' – it may be a scribal emphasis.
2
Identify the three etymologies
The passage presents three possible origins of 'jawhar'. List them in your own words.
The first comes from al-Qamus, the second from 'jahr', the third from 'jawhar' meaning appearance.
3
Understand the transfer of meaning
Notice how the technical term is derived from the lexical meaning: just as stones yield benefits, substance yields accidents.
4
Reflect on why this matters
Consider why a theologian would begin with etymology. It anchors abstract concepts in familiar language.
This connects to the broader method of kalam: using clear definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( في الجواهر الجوهر حجر يستخرج . منه شيء ينتفع به على ما في القاموس نقل الاصطلاح إلى المعنى المذكور لأنه يستخرج يستخرج منه الخواص والأعراض التي ينتفع بها، وقيل مشتق من الجهر بمعنى الظاهر ويحتمل أن يكون من الجوهر بمعنى هيئة الرجل وحسن منظره.
His statement: (On substances): The substance (jawhar) is a stone from which something beneficial is extracted according to what is in al-Qamus; the technical term was transferred to the mentioned meaning because from it are extracted the properties and accidents that are of benefit. And it is said it is derived from 'jahr' meaning apparent, and it is possible that it is from 'jawhar' meaning the appearance of a man and his good countenance.
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage is a lexical explanation of the term 'jawahir' (substances). It provides two possible etymologies. The first, based on al-Qamus, says that 'jawhar' originally meant a stone from which something beneficial is extracted; the technical term was then applied to substance because it bears accidents (like the stone yields benefits). The second etymology suggests it is derived from 'jahr' meaning apparent, because substance is more manifest than accident. A third possibility is that it comes from 'jawhar' meaning the appearance or good countenance of a man.

Key Terms
الجوهر
الجهر
العارض
الاصطلاح
القاموس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The term 'jawhar' is a purely technical term with no linguistic roots.
Correction: It has a clear lexical origin from a stone or from the meaning of apparent.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the first lexical meaning of 'jawhar' mentioned?
A) A type of jewel
B) A stone from which something beneficial is extracted
C) The appearance of a man
D) That which is apparent

Key Takeaway
The kalam term 'jawhar' has multiple plausible lexical origins, reflecting its fundamental role in describing manifest, beneficial entities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that 'jawhar' literally means a stone that yields something beneficial. In your own life, what 'stone' have you been neglecting that, if examined, could yield beneficial insights about your faith or character? Name one thing you will investigate today.
?
The author suggests 'jawhar' may come from 'jahr' (apparent). Are there aspects of your faith that you have kept hidden or obscure, when they should be manifest for others to see? What is one truth you will make more apparent starting tomorrow?
?
The third etymology relates to a man's appearance and good countenance. Do you treat your outward demeanor as a 'substance' from which beneficial qualities should be extracted for others? How can you improve in this area before your next interaction?
